When Ghusl Becomes Obligatory After Menses: The Hanafi Intra-Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on timing of ghusl obligation after menses stops

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on when ghusl becomes obligatory after menstruation cessation and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Ghusl after menses: obligation at cessation for Iraqis, at prayer time for Bukharans – the latter is relied upon.

The obligation of ghusl after menstruation is undisputed, but when exactly does that obligation attach? This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute between Iraqi and Bukharan scholars, examining the reasoning behind each view and the practical consequence for a woman's sin. By understanding this dispute, you will appreciate how regional differences within a madhhab are resolved in favor of one operative position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is ghusl from haid. Its ruling is fard 'ayn (individual obligation). The dispute is not about whether it is obligatory, but when the obligation begins.
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Position 1 (Iraqi Hanafis, al-Karkhi): ghusl is obligatory immediately upon cessation of blood. Position 2 (Bukharan Hanafis): ghusl is obligatory only when prayer time enters after cessation.
3
Analyze the practical consequence
If menses stops after sunrise and she delays ghusl to Zuhr: Iraqis say she sins for delaying, Bukharans say she does not because the obligation of Zuhr has not yet entered at cessation.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (fruit of the difference).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states 'wa huwa al-mukhtar' (and that is the chosen view), referring to the Bukharan position. Therefore, the operative ruling for fatwa is that ghusl becomes obligatory upon wujub al-salah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف المشايخ : هل يجب الغُسل بالانقطاع ووجوب الصلاة، أو بالانقطاع، لا غير؟ فعند الكرخي وعامة العراقيين بالانقطاع، وهو اختيار الشيخ. وعند البخاريين: بوجوب الصلاة، وهو المختار. وفائدته : إذا انقطع بعد طلوع الشمس، وأخرت الغُسل إلى وقت الظهر : فعند العراقيين : تأثم، وعند البخاريين: لا تأثم. والنفاس : كالحيض.
The mashayikh differed: Is ghusl obligatory due to cessation (of menses) and obligation of prayer, or due to cessation only? According to al-Karkhi and most Iraqis, it is due to cessation, and that is the selection of the shaykh. According to the Bukharis, it is due to obligation of prayer, and that is the chosen view. Its practical consequence: If it (menses) ceases after sunrise and she delays ghusl until Zuhr time, then according to the Iraqis she sins, and according to the Bukharis she does not sin. And nifas is like haid.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the precise moment when ghusl becomes obligatory after menstruation ceases. The question is whether the obligation attaches merely upon the cessation of blood (al-intiqāʿ) or upon the obligation of prayer (wujūb al-ṣalāh) after cessation. Al-Karkhī and the majority of Iraqi Hanafis hold that ghusl is obligatory upon cessation itself, regardless of whether prayer time has entered. This is also the choice of the author (al-shaykh). The Bukharan Hanafis (al-Bukhāriyyūn) hold that ghusl is obligatory only when prayer becomes obligatory, i.e., when enough time remains to perform ghusl before the prayer time ends. The relied-upon position (al-mukhtār) is the latter. The practical difference (fāʿidah) is: if menses stops after sunrise and she delays ghusl until Zuhr, according to the Iraqis she incurs sin for delaying the ghusl, whereas according to the Bukharis she does not sin because the obligation of prayer (Zuhr) has not yet entered at the time of cessation. The passage also states that the rulings for nifas (postpartum bleeding) are the same as for haid.

Key Terms
الانقطاع (al-intiqāʿ): Cessation of menstrual blood flow.
البخاريين (al-Bukhāriyyīn): Hanafi scholars from Bukhara, as opposed to Iraqi Hanafis.
وجوب الصلاة (wujūb al-ṣalāh): Obligation of prayer (i.e., the entry of a prayer time).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (al-mukhtār), when does ghusl become obligatory after menstruation ceases?
A) Upon the cessation of blood, regardless of time.
B) When the next prayer time enters.
C) At the time of maghrib.
D) Only after performing wudu.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that ghusl after menses becomes obligatory only when prayer time enters, not immediately upon cessation.

Ghusl for Janabah and Haid: A Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on legal effect of ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether a single ghusl performed after janabah and menstruation covers both impurities, and apply this to an oath scenario.

Core Idea
A single ghusl may or may not cover both impurities; the consequence appears in oaths.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of a single ghusl when a woman is junub and then menstruates before bathing. The difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad affects whether an oath to avoid bathing for janabah is broken by a bath taken after menstruation. Understanding the reasoning behind each position sharpens your ability to analyze ritual impurity and its legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A woman becomes junub, then menstruates, then performs a single ghusl. Does this ghusl count for janabah only, or for both janabah and haid?
The scenario assumes the ghusl is done after the onset of menstruation but before it ends.
2
State the two positions
Abu Yusuf: the ghusl is only for janabah (the first state). Muhammad: the ghusl is for both janabah and haid.
No explicit dalil is given in the text; the reasoning is inferred from principles of ritual purity.
3
Understand the underlying reasoning
Abu Yusuf likely views haid as a distinct impurity that requires a separate intention or a different ghusl. Muhammad likely sees the act of bathing as sufficient for all impurities present, unless a specific intention restricts it.
The text does not elaborate, so this step requires extrapolation from general Hanafi usul.
4
Work through the oath consequence
If she swore not to bathe from 'this janabah', then menstruated and bathed after Zuhr (when haid may have stopped), Abu Yusuf says she breaks the oath because the ghusl addressed janabah. Muhammad says she does not because the ghusl was for both impurities and not solely for janabah.
The key is what the oath prohibits: bathing from janabah specifically.
5
Note the consensus exception
If she bathes while still menstruating (before becoming pure), she breaks the oath by consensus, because the haid is ongoing and the ghusl would not be valid for that state.
This shows that the dispute only applies when the haid has ended or is not relevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أجنبت المرأة، ثم حاضت، فاغتسلت: فعند أبي يوسف: الغُسل من الأول، وهو الجنابة، وعند محمد : هو منهما جميعاً. وفائدته: أنها إذا حلفت لا تغتسل من هذه الجنابة، ثم حاضت، فاغتسلت بعد الظهر حنثت عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد : لا تحنث. وإن اغتسلت قبل أن تطهر من الحيض: حنثت، إجماعاً.
And if a woman becomes junub, then menstruates, and then bathes: according to Abu Yusuf, the ghusl is from the first [state], which is janabah; and according to Muhammad, it is from both. Its practical consequence: if she swore that she would not bathe from this janabah, then menstruated, and then bathed after Zuhr, she would break her oath according to Abu Yusuf, but according to Muhammad she does not break it. And if she bathed before becoming pure from menses, she breaks her oath by consensus.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the legal effect of a single ghusl performed by a woman who was junub and then began menstruating before bathing. According to Abu Yusuf, the ghusl only removes the janabah (major ritual impurity from sexual intercourse or seminal emission), not the haid (menstrual impurity), because the haid is a separate state that persists until the blood stops. Therefore, after the ghusl, she is purified from janabah but remains in haid. According to Muhammad, the ghusl suffices for both impurities simultaneously, because the act of bathing in the state of haid can be intended for both, and the water purifies from all. The practical consequence appears in an oath: if she swore not to bathe from 'this janabah,' then menstruated and bathed after Zuhr (i.e., after the menstruation has likely stopped), according to Abu Yusuf she has violated her oath because she performed a ghusl that addressed the janabah; according to Muhammad she has not violated it because the ghusl was not solely for janabah but also for haid. However, if she bathed while still menstruating (before becoming pure), she breaks the oath by consensus, because the haid is still ongoing and the ghusl is not valid for that state.

Key Terms
الجنابة (al-janābah): State of major ritual impurity due to sexual intercourse or seminal discharge.
الحيض (al-ḥayḍ): Menstrual blood, a state of ritual impurity requiring ghusl after cessation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a woman is junub, then menstruates, and then bathes after Zuhr (assuming her menses have stopped), what happens to her oath not to bathe from janabah?
A) She breaks the oath according to both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
B) She breaks the oath according to Abu Yusuf but not according to Muhammad.
C) She does not break the oath according to either imam.
D) The passage does not address this scenario.

Key Takeaway
The effect of a single ghusl on multiple impurities is disputed within the Hanafi school, affecting the validity of oaths.

Sunnah Ghusl and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Friday Ghusl Timing
Listing of sunnah ghusl occasions and intra-Hanafi dispute on Friday ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the occasions for which ghusl is sunnah muakkadah according to the Hanafi school, and explain the practical consequences of the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and al-Hasan regarding the timing of Friday ghusl.

Core Idea
Ghusl for Friday is sunnah muakkadah, but its basis—for the prayer or the day—affects when it is valid.

The Prophet ﷺ made ghusl sunnah for several occasions: Friday, the two Eids, entering ihram, and the day of Arafah. Among the Hanafi masters, there is a well-known disagreement whether the ghusl for Friday is tied to the congregational prayer (salat al-jumu'ah) or to the day itself. This distinction leads to concrete differences in practice, especially regarding timing and who receives the reward.

Guided Path
1
List the occasions
Identify the four occasions for which ghusl is sunnah muakkadah as mentioned in the text: Friday, the two Eids, entering ihram (for hajj or umrah), and the day of Arafah.
The text uses 'Sanna' indicating the Prophet ﷺ made it sunnah.
2
Identify the dispute
Pinpoint the disagreement: Abu Yusuf says ghusl for Friday is for the prayer, al-Hasan says it is for the day. Both are authoritative within the Hanafi school, but this affects three practical scenarios.
Al-Hasan here is likely al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, a narrator of nawadir.
3
Analyze the three consequences
Work through each scenario: (1) ghusl before dawn, (2) ghusl after prayer before sunset, (3) women performing ghusl. For each, state whether it counts as sunnah according to each imam.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical outcome) is the key to understanding the dispute.
4
Apply analogy to Eids
The text states that ghusl for the two Eids is analogous to Friday ghusl. Consider whether the same dispute would apply for Eid ghusl (though not explicitly discussed here).
Most Hanafi works treat Eid ghusl as sunnah without such a dispute, but the analogy is made explicit.
5
Note the grammatical rule
Understand the pronunciation of 'ghusl/ghasl' in idafa: damm (ghusl) when added to something other than the object washed, fatha (ghasl) when added to the thing washed.
This is a side point but helps avoid common mispronunciations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم الغسل للجمعة والعيدين والإحرام وعرفة. وسواء كان إحرام حج أو عمرة. (و) كذا يوم عرفة؛ للوقوف. واختلف أصحابنا هل غسل الجمعة للصلاة، أو لليوم؟ قال أبو يوسف: للصلاة. وقال الحسن: لليوم. وفائدته: إذا اغتسل قبل طلوع الفجر، ولم يُحدث حتى صلى الجمعة: يكون آتياً بالسنة عند أبي يوسف، وعند الحسن: لا. وكذا إذا اغتسل بعد صلاة الجمعة قبل الغروب: يكون آتياً بالسنة عند الحسن، خلافاً لأبي يوسف. ولو اغتسلت المرأة: لا تنال فضيلة الغسل عند أبي يوسف؛ لأنه لا جمعة عليها، وعند الحسن: تنالها. والغسل للعيدين: بمنزلة الغسل للجمعة. واعلم أنه يقال: غسل الجمعة، وغسل الجنابة: بضم الغين، وغسل الميت، وغسل الثوب بفتحها، وضابطه أنك إذا أضفت إلى المغسول: فتحت، وإذا أضفت إلى غيره: ضممت.
The Messenger of Allah ﷺ made Sunnah the ghusl for Friday, the two Eids, Ihram, and Arafah. Whether it is Ihram for Hajj or Umrah. And similarly the day of Arafah for standing (wuquf). Our companions differed: Is the ghusl of Friday for the prayer or for the day? Abu Yusuf said: for the prayer. Al-Hasan said: for the day. Its practical consequence: if he bathes before dawn and does not break wudu until he prays Friday, he has performed the Sunnah according to Abu Yusuf, but according to al-Hasan he has not. Similarly, if he bathes after Friday prayer before sunset, he has performed the Sunnah according to al-Hasan, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And if a woman bathes, she does not attain the virtue of the ghusl according to Abu Yusuf because Friday prayer is not obligatory upon her, but according to al-Hasan she attains it. And the ghusl for the two Eids is like the ghusl for Friday. And know that it is said: ghusl (with damm) for Friday, and ghusl (with damm) for janabah, but ghusl (with fatha) of the deceased, and ghusl (with fatha) of the garment. The rule: if you add (idafa) to the thing being washed, you say it with fatha; if you add to something else, you say it with damm.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage begins by listing the occasions for which Ghusl is Sunnah according to the Hanafi school: Friday, the two Eids, entering the state of Ihram (whether for Hajj or Umrah), and the day of Arafah for the standing (wuquf). Then it presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and al-Hasan (likely al-Hasan ibn Ziyad al-Lu'lu'i, a principal narrator of Nawadir) regarding the basis of the Friday ghusl: is it for the Friday prayer (salat al-jumu'ah) itself, or for the day (al-yawm) of Friday? Abu Yusuf holds it is for the prayer, while al-Hasan holds it is for the day. The practical consequences (fā'idah) are threefold: (1) If a person performs ghusl before dawn (fajr) on Friday and maintains wudu until he prays Jumu'ah, according to Abu Yusuf he has fulfilled the Sunnah since the ghusl is for the prayer and he did it before that prayer, whereas according to al-Hasan he has not, because the ghusl is for the day and should be performed after dawn. (2) If he performs ghusl after the Friday prayer but before sunset, al-Hasan considers it valid (as it is still Friday), but Abu Yusuf does not. (3) Regarding women: since Jumu'ah prayer is not obligatory on women, does the Sunnah ghusl still apply? Abu Yusuf says they do not attain the virtue because it is tied to the prayer, while al-Hasan says they do because it is for the day. The passage further states that the ghusl for the two Eids is analogous to the ghusl for Friday. Finally, it gives a grammatical rule: the word 'ghusl' is pronounced with a damm (ghusl) when it is in idafa (construct) to something that is not the object of washing (e.g., ghusl al-jumu'ah, ghusl al-janabah), but with a fatha (ghasl) when it is in idafa to the thing being washed (e.g., ghasl al-mayyit, ghasl al-thawb).

Key Terms
الغسل (ghusl/ghasl): Ritual bath; pronunciation varies by idafa.
الجمعة (al-jumuʿah): Friday congregational prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Hasan (al-Hasan ibn Ziyad), when must the ghusl for Friday be performed to be considered Sunnah?
A) Before dawn.
B) After dawn and before sunset.
C) Only after the Friday prayer.
D) At any time on Friday.

Key Takeaway
The sunnah ghusl for Friday is sunnah muakkadah, but its timing and applicability depend on whether it is considered for the prayer or for the day—a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with practical consequences.

The Purpose of the Friday Bath: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Lists recommended baths and details the intra-Hanafi dispute on Friday bath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on the purpose of the Friday bath and apply their practical consequences to timing and women's participation.

Core Idea
The Friday bath is a confirmed sunnah, but does it belong to the prayer or to the day? The answer changes timing and who benefits.

The Friday bath (ghusl al-jumu'ah) is a recommended practice, but its precise character sparked an internal Hanafi debate. Abu Yusuf tied it to the prayer itself, while al-Hasan ibn Ziyad tied it to the day of Friday. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its practical implications for when to bathe and whether women share in the reward.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed ruling
First, note that both Abu Yusuf and al-Hasan agree that ghusl on Friday is a sunnah mu'akkadah. The dispute is about its purpose, not its desirability.
2
Contrast the two positions
Abu Yusuf: the bath is for the prayer, so it must be performed after the time of Jumu'ah begins (after sunrise). Al-Hasan: the bath is for the day, so it is valid from dawn until sunset.
3
Apply to three scenarios
Work through the practical differences: (1) bathing before dawn, (2) bathing after the prayer before sunset, (3) a woman bathing on Friday. Note how each imam's ruling changes the outcome.
4
Note the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., Ibn Abidin) prefer al-Hasan's view as more cautious and practical. The bath is thus valid from break of dawn.
5
Learn the linguistic rule
The passage ends with a pronunciation rule: when the word for washing is added (idafa) to the thing washed, pronounce it 'ghasl' (fatha); otherwise 'ghusl' (damma). This applies to ghusl al-janabah vs ghasl al-mayyit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
والعيدين، والإحرام، وعَرَفَةَ .
والعيدين والإحرام).
سواء كان إحرام حج أو عمرة.
(و) كذا يوم (عرفة) ؛ للوقوف.
واختلف أصحابنا هل غُسل الجمعة للصلاة، أو لليوم؟
قال أبو يوسف : للصلاة.
وقال الحسن : لليوم.
وفائدته : إذا اغتسل قبل طلوع الفجر، ولم يُحدث حتى صلى الجمعة : يكون آتياً بالسنة عند أبي يوسف، وعند الحسن: لا. وكذا إذا اغتسل بعد صلاة الجمعة قبل الغروب: يكون آتياً بالسنة عند الحسن، خلافاً لأبي يوسف.
ولو اغتسلت المرأة : لا تنال فضيلة الغُسل عند أبي يوسف؛ لأنه لا جمعة عليها، وعند الحسن: تنالها.
-
والغسل للعيدين: بمنزلة الغُسل للجمعة.
واعلم أنه يقال : غُسلُ الجمعة، وغسل الجنابة: بضم الغين، وغسل الميت، وغسل الثوب بفتحها، وضابطه أنك إذا أضفت إلى المغسول: فتحت، وإذا أضفت إلى غيره : ضَمَمْتَ.
Book of Purification. And the two 'Ids, and Ihram, and 'Arafa. And the two 'Ids and Ihram. Whether it is ihram for Hajj or 'Umrah. And likewise the day of 'Arafa; due to standing. Our companions differed: is the ghusl of Friday for the prayer or for the day? Abu Yusuf said: for the prayer. And al-Hasan said: for the day. Its benefit: if one bathes before dawn and does not break his wudu until he prays Jumu'ah, he will have performed the sunnah according to Abu Yusuf, but according to al-Hasan, no. Likewise, if he bathes after the Jumu'ah prayer before sunset, he will have performed the sunnah according to al-Hasan, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And if a woman bathes, she does not attain the virtue of the bath according to Abu Yusuf because Jumu'ah is not obligatory upon her, but according to al-Hasan, she attains it. - And the bath for the two 'Ids is like the bath for Friday. And know that it is said: ghusl of Friday and ghusl of janabah with a dammah on the ghayn, and ghasl of the deceased and ghasl of the garment with a fatha, and its rule is: if you add it to the thing being washed, you open it, and if you add it to something else, you close it.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah begins by listing the recommended baths for Friday, the two Eids, entering ihram (for Hajj or Umrah), and standing at Arafa. The commentator then introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the nature of the Friday bath. Abu Yusuf holds that it is for the prayer itself, so it must be performed after the time of Jumu'ah begins (after sunrise), and if one bathes before dawn it does not count as the sunnah. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad holds that it is for the day of Friday, so it can be performed from dawn onwards, and even after the prayer until sunset, it is valid. Another difference is regarding women: since Jumu'ah prayer is not obligatory on them, Abu Yusuf says they do not obtain the virtue of the bath, while al-Hasan says they do because the day itself is the occasion. The commentator then applies the same ruling to the two Eids. Finally, a linguistic rule clarifies the pronunciation: the word for ritual bath (ghusl) is pronounced with a damma on the ghayn when referring to the act of purification, while the word for washing a corpse or garment (ghasl) is with a fatha. The rule is: if the word is added (idafa) to the thing being washed, it takes fatha; otherwise, damma.

Key Terms
غسل (ghusl / ghasl): The Arabic word for washing; in this context, it refers to the ritual bath (ghusl) for purification from major impurity (janabah) or recommended baths.
الحسن (al-Hasan): Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad al-Lu'lu'i, a prominent Hanafi scholar and companion of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. He transmitted many narrations from Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, when can a person perform the Friday bath and still obtain its sunnah?
A) Only after the time of Jumu'ah prayer begins
B) From the break of dawn until sunset
C) Only before the Friday prayer
D) Only after the Friday prayer

Key Takeaway
The Friday bath is a sunnah mu'akkadah; the preferred Hanafi view (al-Hasan) ties it to the day, making it valid from dawn and inclusive of women.

Madhi and Wady: Ruling and Reasoning
States and explains the ruling for madhi and wady, addressing a potential objection and two scenarios for wady

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on madhi and wady, explain the reason for the author's explicit mention, and apply the ruling to scenarios involving urinary incontinence.

Core Idea
Madhi and wady require only wudu; they are implicitly covered but explicitly stated to prevent error.

This passage from Al-Jawharah al-Nayrah clarifies the taharah rulings for two often confused fluids: madhi (pre-ejaculatory fluid) and wady (prostatic fluid). The matn states that neither requires ghusl but both necessitate wudu. The commentator defends this explicit mention by addressing why a general principle is insufficient and provides concrete scenarios for wady.

Guided Path
1
Define the terms
Identify the definitions of madhi and wady as given in the text: madhi is a thin white fluid during foreplay; wady is a thick yellow fluid after urination.
Both are pronounced with a lightened ya.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is clear: no ghusl, only wudu is required for both. This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The commentator anticipates an objection: if the general 'everything exiting the two passages' already covers them, why mention them? The response uses the analogy of implicit vs explicit inclusion in sales.
Understand the difference between implicit and explicit inclusion.
4
Apply to scenarios
Two scenarios for wady: (1) person with urinary incontinence passes wady – wudu is for the wady; (2) person who urinated, did wudu, then passes wady – fresh wudu is required.
These scenarios show the practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس في المذي، والوَدْي غُسل، وفيهما الوضوء.
قوله : (وليس في المذي والوَدْي غُسل، وفيهما الوضوء). المَذْيُ: ماءً أبيض رقيق يخرج عند الملاعبة.
والوَدْي: ماءً أصفر غليظ يخرج بعد البول، وكلاهما بتخفيف الياء. وقوله : وفيهما الوضوء: فإن قيل : قد استفيد وجوب الوضوء بقوله: كل ما خرج من السبيلين: فلم أعادهما؟
قلنا: إنما دخلا هناك ضمناً، لا قصداً، ومن الأشياء ما يدخل ضمناً، ولا يدخل قصداً كبيع الشرب والطريق، فربما يُتوهم أنهما يدخلان ضمناً، لا قصداً؟
فإن قلت: وكيف يتصور الوضوء من الوَدْي وهو قد وجب بالبول السابق؟
قلت: يتصوّر فيمن به سلس البول إذا أودى: يتوضأ، ويكون وضوءه من الودي خاصة.
ويتصور أيضاً فيمن بال وتوضأ ثم أودى : فإنه يتوضأ من الودي.
There is no ghusl for madhi and wady, and there is wudu for both. His words: (There is no ghusl for madhi and wady, and there is wudu for both). Al-madhy: a thin white fluid that comes out during foreplay. And al-wady: a thick yellow fluid that comes out after urine, and both are with a lightened ya. And his words: 'and there is wudu for both': if it is said: the obligation of wudu is understood from his statement: 'everything that exits from the two passages' so why did he repeat them? We say: they are included there implicitly, not explicitly, and there are things that are included implicitly and not explicitly, like the sale of the drinking right and the right of way, so perhaps it might be misunderstood that they are included implicitly but not explicitly? If you say: how can wudu be conceived from wady when it is already necessitated by the previous urine? I say: it is conceivable in the case of one with urinary incontinence who passes wady: he performs wudu, and his wudu is from the wady specifically. And it is also conceivable in the case of one who urinated and performed wudu and then passed wady: he performs wudu from the wady.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents a clear fiqh ruling from the matn of al-Quduri: madhi (pre-ejaculatory fluid) and wady (prostatic fluid) do not require a full bath (ghusl), but only wudu. The commentator defines both terms: madhi is a thin white fluid emitted during foreplay, and wady is a thick yellowish fluid that comes out after urination. Both words are pronounced with a lightened ya (no tashdid). The commentator then addresses a potential objection: if the general rule 'everything that exits from the two passages invalidates wudu' already implies wudu is required, why does the author explicitly mention madhi and wady? The answer is that these two fluids are included implicitly in the general rule, but the author wants to emphasize them because people might mistakenly think they are not included, similar to the case in sales where the right to drink water (shurb) and the right of way (tariq) are included implicitly in a land sale but are not explicitly mentioned. A further question arises: how can one need wudu from wady if the preceding urine already necessitated wudu? The commentator provides two scenarios: (1) a person with urinary incontinence (sals al-bawl) who passes wady must perform wudu specifically for the wady; (2) a person who urinated, performed wudu, and then passed wady must perform a fresh wudu because the wady is a new hadath that occurred after the wudu.

Key Terms
المذي (al-madhy): A thin white fluid that emerges during foreplay or sexual arousal, which requires only wudu according to the Hanafi school.
الودي (al-wady): A thick yellow fluid that comes out after urination, which also requires only wudu. It is often confused with pre-ejaculatory fluid.
سلس البول (sals al-bawl): Urinary incontinence, a condition where urine leaks continuously or uncontrollably. In fiqh, the rules for wudu are relaxed for such a person: they must perform wudu for each prayer time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author explicitly mention madhi and wady in the text if they are already covered by the general rule 'everything exiting the two passages'?
A) Because they were not included in the general rule
B) To emphasize that they are included and avoid misunderstanding
C) Because they are exceptions to the general rule
D) To show that they require ghusl, not wudu

Key Takeaway
Madhi and wady are minor impurities requiring only wudu; their explicit mention in the text prevents misinterpretation of the general rule.

Introduction to Purification and Water Types
Opening of a new fiqh section on purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two main categories of purification in Hanafi fiqh and identify the opening topic of al-Quduri's chapter on purification.

Core Idea
Purification from ritual impurity is a key prerequisite for prayer, starting with the water that makes it valid.

This lesson opens the section on purification (taharah) from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The phrase 'الماء الذي يتطهر به' (the water by which one purifies) signals the beginning of a detailed discussion on the types of water suitable for wudu and ghusl. Understanding this foundation is essential before diving into the rules of ritual purity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opening phrase
Read the Arabic: 'الماء الذي يتطهر به'. This is the title of a new sub-section. Note that the word 'يتطهر' indicates the act of purification using water.
2
Recognize the broader context
The second line 'والطهارة من الأحداث' is a quotation from the matn, linking water purification to the removal of ritual impurities (ahdath). This sets the scope of the chapter: both the purifying agent (water) and the impurity (hadath) are introduced.
3
Distinguish the two branches of taharah
Hanafi fiqh divides taharah into (1) taharah from hadath (ritual impurity) — achieved by wudu or ghusl — and (2) taharah from najasah (physical impurity) — achieved by washing. This passage focuses on the first branch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماء الذي يتطهر به :
قوله: (والطهارة من الأحداث).
The water by which one purifies. His words: (And purification from ritual impurities).
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This short passage marks the beginning of a new section in the matn of al-Quduri. The first line announces the topic: the water that can be used for purification (taharah). The second line is a quotation from the matn: 'And purification from ritual impurities (ahdath).' This introduces the broader topic of the different types of impurities (hadath asghar and hadath akbar) and the means of purification (wudu, ghusl, tayammum). The discussion will likely cover the categories of water (e.g., pure, impure, etc.) and the conditions for its use.

Key Terms
الطهارة (al-taharah): Purification from ritual impurity; includes both wudu and ghusl.
الأحداث (al-ahdath): Ritual impurities that require purification through wudu or ghusl. Singular: hadath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main topic introduced by the phrase 'الماء الذي يتطهر به'?
A) The types of ritual baths
B) The water suitable for purification
C) The rules of wudu
D) The categories of impurities

Key Takeaway
The purification chapter begins by identifying the essential purifying agent (water) and its categories before dealing with the impurities themselves.

Permissible Water for Purification: An Overview
Listing pure water sources and prohibition of squeezed plant juices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on permissible water for wudu and ghusl, and identify why squeezed plant juices are invalid.

Core Idea
Only absolute (mutlaq) water purifies; squeezed liquids are not water and thus invalid for wudu.

We often assume any clear liquid can be used for wudu, but the Hanafi school strictly limits it to water that is unchanged in its essential nature. This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah lists the sources and explains the key principle: if the liquid is extracted by squeezing, it is no longer mutlaq water and cannot remove impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage is about purification (taharah) from hadath (minor and major impurities). It rules that purification is permissible (jā'iz) with certain waters, and not permissible with squeezed juices.
The author says 'jā'izah' (permissible) not 'wājibah' (obligatory) because if water is absent or time is narrow, tayammum takes precedence.
2
List the permissible water sources
The matn names five sources: rainwater (mā' al-samā'), stream water (mā' al-awdiya), spring water (mā' al-'uyūn), well water (mā' al-ābār), and sea water (mā' al-biḥār).
3
Identify the prohibited category
The matn prohibits using 'what is squeezed from trees and fruits' (mā iʿtuṣira min al-shajar wa al-thamar). This includes all plant juices extracted by pressing.
The commentator notes that if the liquid flows naturally (e.g., from a cut fruit), it is permissible, because the prohibition is due to the act of squeezing.
4
Understand the reason for prohibition
Squeezing changes the water from its original state (mutlaq) to a mixed/derived liquid. For wudu, the water must remain absolute (mā' mutlaq) – unchanged in its essence. Squeezed liquids are not considered water.
The Hanafi principle: water becomes invalid if it loses the name 'water' due to mixing with a predominant substance.
5
Apply the ruling to a new case
If fruit juice flows naturally (e.g., from a cut orange) without squeezing, the Hanafi school allows using it for wudu (though it might be makruh due to waste). The key is the absence of iʿtiṣār (squeezing).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطهارة من الأحداث جائزة بماء السماء، والأودية، والعيون، والآبار، والبحار. ولا تجوز الطهارة بما اعتصر من الشجر، والثمر . طهارة الأحداث هي الوضوء والغسل، والألف واللام للعهد، أي الأحداث التي سبق ذكرها من البول والغائط والحيض والنفاس وغيرها. * قوله : (جائزة بماء السماء). ولم يقل: واجبةٌ؛ لأن معناه : إذا اجتمعت هذه المياه، أو انفرد أحدهما، ولم يتضيق الوقت، وإلا فهي واجبة. وقوله : من الأحداث : ليس هو على التخصيص؛ لأنه لما كان مزيلاً للأحداث : كان مزيلاً للأنجاس بالطريق الأولى. قوله: (وماء البحار) : إنما قال: وماء البحار، ولم يقل: والبحار: رداً لقول من يقول: إنه ليس بماء، حتى «حكي عن ابن عمر رضي عنهما أنه قال : التيمم أحبُّ إليَّ منه»(۱). قوله : (ولا تجوز الطهارة بما اعتصر من الشجر، والثَّمَر). بالله بالقصر، على أن ما بمعنى الذي وإن كان يصح بمعنى الممدود؛ لأن المنقول: هو الموصول. - وإنما قيد بالاعتصار؛ لأنه لو سال بنفسه : جاز الوضوء به، إلا أن
And purification from impurities is permissible with rainwater, stream water, spring water, well water, and sea water. And purification is not permissible with what is squeezed from trees and fruits. Purification from impurities is wudu and ghusl, and the definite article indicates the specific impurities previously mentioned – urine, feces, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, etc. * His statement: (permissible with rainwater). He did not say: obligatory; because it means: when these waters are available or one of them is available, and time is not narrow; otherwise it is obligatory. And his statement: from impurities: it is not specific; because since it removes impurities, it removes najasah a fortiori. His statement: (and sea water): he said 'and water of the seas' and not 'and the seas' – as a refutation of the one who says it is not water, such that it is narrated from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with both) that he said: 'Tayammum is more beloved to me than it.' His statement: (and purification is not permissible with what is squeezed from trees and fruits) – the word 'ma' is with a shortened alif, indicating the relative pronoun 'which', although it could be with a long alif; because the transmitted form is the relative pronoun. And he restricted it to squeezing; because if it flows by itself, wudu with it is permissible, except that...
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on water for purification. The matn lists permissible water sources: rainwater, stream water, spring water, well water, and sea water. It then prohibits purification with squeezed plant juices. The commentator explains: the phrase 'purification from impurities' refers specifically to wudu and ghusl for the impurities previously mentioned (urine, feces, etc.). He clarifies that the author said 'permissible' not 'obligatory' because wudu is only obligatory when water is available and time is not tight; otherwise, tayammum may be used. The phrase 'from impurities' is general, so it also covers removal of najasah (impurities on the body) by priority. Regarding sea water, the author explicitly says 'water of the seas' to refute those who claim sea water is not pure (narrated from Ibn Umar preferring tayammum). Finally, squeezed liquids from trees and fruits are not valid for wudu because they are not absolute water (mā' mutlaq). However, if the liquid flows naturally (not squeezed), it is permissible to use, implying that the prohibition is only due to the act of squeezing which changes it from its original state.

Key Terms
ماء السماء (mā' al-samā'): Rainwater, considered pure and purifying.
اعتصار (i‘tiṣār): Squeezing or extracting liquid from a solid source, like pressing fruit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Well water

D) Sea water

Key Takeaway
Only absolute water (mā' mutlaq) — whether from rain, wells, seas, etc. — can be used for purification; liquids derived by squeezing are not water and invalidate wudu.

Valid Water Sources and Invalid Squeezed Juices for Purification
Defines valid water sources and invalidates squeezed tree/fruit juices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five valid water sources for purification according to the Hanafi school and explain why squeezed fruit/tree juices are invalid.

Core Idea
Five waters are valid; squeezed juices are invalid—only natural water purifies.

This passage from the Mukhtasar al-Quduri lists the water sources permissible for wudu and ghusl. While the matn is concise, the commentary reveals why the author chose 'jā'izah' over 'wājibah' and which waters are excluded. Understanding these rulings forms the basis of taharah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five valid water sources
List the five types mentioned: rainwater, river water, spring water, well water, sea water.
2
Understand the ruling's nuance
The word 'jā'izah' indicates permissibility, not obligation. When is it obligatory? When time is tight (e.g., salah time ending).
The commentator clarifies that if water is scarce or time constrained, purification becomes wajib.
3
Recognize the invalidity of squeezed juices
The matn explicitly forbids using liquid extracted by squeezing from trees or fruits. This includes grape juice, etc.
The key is the act of squeezing (iʿtiṣār).
4
Differentiate natural flow from squeezing
The commentator notes that if liquid flows by itself (e.g., sap from a cut branch without pressing), it is permissible. Only the product of intentional squeezing is invalid.
This is a critical distinction for practical application.
5
Address the seawater dispute
The commentator mentions that some scholars (like Ibn Umar) deemed seawater unsuitable, but the Hanafi school rejects that view and includes it as valid.
This shows the school's commitment to the general principle that all natural water purifies.

Book of Purification. Purification is permissible with rainwater, river water, spring water, well water, and sea water. And purification is not permissible with what is squeezed from trees and fruits. Purification of minor impurities: it is wudu and ghusl, and the definite article is for previous mention, i.e., the impurities previously mentioned from urine, feces, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and others. He said: 'Permissible with rainwater' – He did not say 'obligatory' because its meaning is: when these waters are gathered or one of them is found and the time is not restricted, otherwise it is obligatory. His statement: 'from impurities' is not for specification, because since it removes impurities, it removes filth by primary derivation. His statement: 'and sea water' – he only said 'sea water' and not 'seas' as a rejection of the one who says it is not water, such that it is narrated from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them both) that he said: 'Tayammum is more beloved to me than it.' Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of al-Quduri with commentary from the sharh. It first states the general rule: purification (wudu and ghusl) is permissible with five types of water: rainwater, river water, spring water, well water, and sea water. The commentator explains that the author used the word 'jā'izah' (permissible) rather than 'wājibah' (obligatory) because the ruling depends on the situation: if water is available and time is not tight, it is permissible (mubah); but if time is restricted (e.g., salah time is about to end), purification with water becomes obligatory. The comment on 'from impurities' clarifies that the water's role in removing impurities applies equally to filth (najāsah) by primary analogy. The discussion of sea water quotes a narration from Ibn Umar preferring tayammum over sea water, but the Hanafi position is that sea water is valid for purification. The second ruling prohibits purification with any liquid squeezed from trees or fruits (e.g., grape juice). The commentator notes that the word 'ma' (what) is a relative pronoun, and the prohibition is specifically for squeezed juices, not for liquids that flow naturally (like fresh tree sap that flows without pressure), which would be permissible.

Key Terms
اعتصار (iʿtiṣār): The act of squeezing or pressing, specifically to extract liquid from tree bark or fruit.
جائزة (jāʾizah): Permissible or allowed, here indicating the permissibility of using these waters for purification.
ماء السماء (māʾ al-samāʾ): Rainwater, literally 'water of the sky'.

Water Dominated by Another Substance: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on water dominated by another substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on water purity regarding volume vs. properties, and state the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
For water mixed with a foreign liquid, validity depends on volume: less than half valid, half or more invalid.

Even within the Hanafi school, there is an important difference of opinion concerning when water mixed with another substance remains valid for purification. This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah presents the views of Abu Yusuf (volume) and Muhammad al-Shaybani (properties), and concludes that the correct view is that volume is the determining factor. Understanding this ikhtilaf helps you see how Hanafi jurists weigh criteria and how later authorities settled the dispute.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the relied-upon view: water mixed with a foreign liquid is valid for purification if the mixture is less than half; if half or more, it is invalid.
This is the position of Abu Yusuf and the preferred view.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Muhammad al-Shaybani considered the change in properties (color, taste, smell) as the criterion. Changing one property is still valid; changing two or three invalidates.
Muhammad's view is also transmitted but not the mufta bihi.
3
Follow the opponent's reasoning
The basis for Muhammad's view is that the purpose of water is to be pure and purifying; if its properties change, it is no longer 'water' in the customary sense.
The text does not elaborate the dalil, but it is understood.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The text reconciles by noting that if the foreign substance is of the same genre as water (like gourd water), then volume is the criterion, aligning with Abu Yusuf. This suggests that the ikhtilaf may be narrowed.
The reconciliation is not a full resolution but a context-based harmonization.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under the volume view, a small amount of juice mixed in water remains valid; under the properties view, if it changes color, it may be invalid. A mufti must follow the mufta bihi.
This affects daily practice for Muslims performing wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
ولا بماء غَلَبَ عليه غيره
* قوله: (ولا بماء غَلَبَ عليه غيره).
اختلفوا فيه : هل الغلبة بالأجزاء، أو بالأوصاف؟
ففي الهداية : بالأجزاء، وهو الصحيح.
وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية: محمد اعتبر اللون، وأبو يوسف اعتبر الأجزاء.
وأشار الشيخ إلى أن المعتبر بالأوصاف.
والأصح أن المعتبر بالأجزاء، وهو أن المخالط إذا كان مائعاً: فما دون النصف جائز ، فإن كان النصف أو أكثر: لا يجوز.
ومحمد اعتبر الأوصاف: إن غير الثلاثةَ : لا يجوز.
وإن غير واحداً: جاز.
وإن غير اثنين: فكذا لا يجوز ، على الصحيح.
والتوفيق بينهما : إن كان مائعاً جنسه جنس الماء، كماء الدباء: فالعبرة
للأجزاء، كما قال أبو يوسف.
(١) قال ابن عابدين ٦٠٤/١ : من راجع كتب المذهب: وجد أكثرها على عدم
الجواز. اهـ، وينظر اللباب ٣٣/٢.
(٢) وفي نسخة مكة من السراج الوهاج يجوز. قلت لكن سيأتي بعد قليل: أنه
لا يجوز عند القدوري، وأن الصحيح أنه يجوز، وكأن في المسألة خلافاً.
Book of Purification. And not with water that is dominated by something else. He said: 'And not with water that is dominated by something else.' They differed concerning it: is dominance by volume or by properties? In al-Hidayah: by volume, and that is correct. In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah: Muhammad considered color, and Abu Yusuf considered volume. The Shaykh indicated that what is considered is properties. The most correct is that volume is considered, and that if the mixing substance is a liquid: less than half is permissible, if half or more, not permissible. And Muhammad considered properties: if it changes all three (color, taste, smell), not permissible; if it changes one, permissible; if it changes two, likewise not permissible, according to the correct view. Reconciliation between them: if the mixing substance is of the same genre as water, like gourd water, then the criterion is volume, as Abu Yusuf said. (1) Ibn Abidin said 1/604: Whoever reviews the books of the madhhab finds most of them on non-permissibility. End. And see al-Lubab 2/33. (2) And in the Makkan copy of al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: it is permissible. I say, but it will come later shortly: that is not permissible according to al-Quduri, and that the correct is that it is permissible, as if there is a difference in the issue.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding water that has been mixed with another substance to the point of being 'dominated' (ghalaba). Two criteria are discussed: volume (ajzā') and properties (awsāf, i.e., color, taste, smell). The author, following al-Hidayah, declares the correct view is consideration of volume: if the foreign substance is less than half, the water remains valid for purification; if half or more, it is invalid. Muhammad al-Shaybani is reported to consider properties: change of all three properties invalidates, change of two invalidates, change of one still permits. The text attempts a reconciliation: if the foreign substance is of a type similar to water (e.g., water of gourd, which is watery and similar in nature), then volume is the criterion as per Abu Yusuf. A footnote from Ibn Abidin notes that most later Hanafi texts adopt non-permissibility (i.e., stricter view), but there is also a copy of al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that permits. The passage shows that even within the Hanafi school, there is nuance, and the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that volume is the determining factor.

Key Terms
الغلبة (al-ghalabah): Dominance of a foreign substance in water, whether by volume or by alteration of properties (color, taste, smell).
الأجزاء (al-ajzāʾ): The volume or quantity of the substance mixed into water.
الأوصاف (al-awṣāf): The three properties of water: color, taste, and smell.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view, when is water mixed with another liquid invalid for purification?
A) When any property (color, taste, smell) changes.
B) When the mixture is half or more of the total.
C) When the mixture is less than half.
D) Only when both color and taste change.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position considers volume: water mixed with a foreign liquid remains valid if the mixture is less than half.

Criterion of Predominance: Parts vs. Attributes
Intra-Hanafi commentary on predominance criterion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi criteria for when water is considered overcome, apply the compromise reconciliation, and identify the practical consequence for a given mixture.

Core Idea
Water is overcome when foreign substance dominates either by quantity or by changing all three attributes; the criterion depends on genus.

This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute on what makes water 'overcome' by another substance: is it the quantity (parts) or the change in properties (attributes)? The commentator presents both views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, then reconciles them by considering the genus of the mixture. Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly applying the ruling that such water cannot be used for purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Start with al-Quduri's statement: 'And not with water over which something else has overcome.' This means such water is not permissible for purification. The question is: when is it considered overcome?
The ruling is clear; the dispute is about the criterion.
2
Contrast the two criteria
Examine the two views: Abu Yusuf says predominance by parts (half or more of the mixture), Muhammad says by attributes (change in color, taste, or smell). Note the thresholds: half or more for parts; one attribute change is okay, two or three is not.
Al-Hidayah declares the parts view as sahih, but al-Quduri chose attributes.
3
Reconcile the views
The commentator reconciles: if the foreign substance is of the same genus as water (e.g., plant juices), use the parts criterion; if different genus (e.g., milk), use attributes. This preserves both imams' insights in their proper contexts.
This reconciliation is a later synthesis, not from the early imams themselves.
4
Apply to examples
Work through the examples: pumpkin water (same genus) – half or more makes it invalid; milk (different genus) – only if all three attributes change. Also note the figure of laf wa nashr linking squeezed juices and mixtures.
The commentator uses the Quranic verse to illustrate the rhetorical structure.

Source Text

الحلواني اختار أنه لا يجوز؛ لأنه يُطلق عليه ماء الشجر
* قوله: (ولا بماء غَلَبَ عليه غيره).
اختلفوا فيه : هل الغلبة بالأجزاء، أو بالأوصاف؟
ففي الهداية : بالأجزاء، وهو الصحيح.

وأشار الشيخ إلى أن المعتبر بالأوصاف.
والأصح أن المعتبر بالأجزاء، وهو أن المخالط إذا كان مائعاً: فما دون النصف جائز ، فإن كان النصف أو أكثر: لا يجوز.
ومحمد اعتبر الأوصاف: إن غير الثلاثةَ : لا يجوز.
وإن غير واحداً: جاز.
وإن غير اثنين: فكذا لا يجوز ، على الصحيح.
والتوفيق بينهما : إن كان مائعاً جنسه جنس الماء، كماء الدباء: فالعبرة للأجزاء، كما قال أبو يوسف.

And not with water over which something else has overcome. Al-Halwani chose that it is not permissible because it is called tree water. * His statement: (And not with water over which something else has overcome). They differed in it: Is the predominance by parts or by attributes? In al-Hidayah: by parts, and it is correct. In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah: Muhammad considered color, and Abu Yusuf considered parts. The Shaykh indicated that the considered is by attributes. And the most correct is that the considered is by parts, and that the mixture, if it is liquid: what is less than half is permissible; if it is half or more, it is not permissible. And Muhammad considered attributes: if it changed all three [color, taste, smell], it is not permissible; if it changed one, it is permissible; if it changed two, then likewise it is not permissible, according to what is correct. And the reconciliation between them: if the liquid's genus is the same as water's genus, like pumpkin water, then the criterion is by parts, as Abu Yusuf said, so it takes it out of the nature of water, such as drinks, vinegar, bean water, and broth. And if its genus is different from water's genus, like milk, then the criterion is by attributes, as Muhammad said. And the Shaykh chose Muhammad's statement as he said: 'So it changed one of its attributes.' * His statement: (so it takes it out of the nature of water): And its nature is thinness, flow, and quenching thirst. * His statement: (such as drinks): that is, made from fruits, like pomegranate drink. Then the Shaykh considered in this the figure of 'laf wa nashr' (scattering and gathering), so his statement: squeezed from trees is a scattering, and likewise with water over which something else has overcome is also a scattering. And his statement: such as drinks is an explanation of what is squeezed from trees and fruits. And his statement: (and vinegar): if it is mixed with water, then it is from what overcomes it; if it is pure, then it is from what is squeezed from fruit. And his statement: (and broth) is an explanation of what overcomes it. An analogy to this is the saying of the Most High: 'And of His mercy He has made for you the night and the day that you may rest therein and seek of His bounty' [28:73]. So His statement: 'that you may rest therein' refers to the night, and 'and seek of His bounty' refers to the day. * His statement: (and bean water): intended is cooked, such that when it cools, it thickens...
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding water that has been overcome by another substance (mā' ghalaba 'alayhi ghayruh). The main ruling, as found in al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, is that such water cannot be used for purification (tahārah). The commentator explains the ikhtilāf (difference of opinion) among Hanafi imams on how to determine when water is considered 'overcome'. There are two criteria: predominance in parts (ajzā')—i.e., the quantity of the foreign substance relative to water—and predominance in attributes (awṣāf)—i.e., change in color, taste, or smell. According to al-Hidāyah, the correct view is by parts, meaning that if the foreign substance is half or more of the mixture, the water is no longer pure; if less than half, it remains permissible. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybānī considered attributes: if all three attributes are changed, the water is not permissible; if only one is changed, it is permissible; if two are changed, it is also not permissible. The commentator then presents a reconciliation between the two views: if the foreign substance is of the same genus as water (e.g., plant juices like pumpkin water), the criterion is by parts (Abū Yūsuf's view); if it is of a different genus (e.g., milk), the criterion is by attributes (Muhammad's view). Al-Quduri (the Shaykh) chose Muhammad's view, as indicated by his phrase 'it changed one of its attributes.' The passage then explains the phrase 'takes it out of the nature of water,' where the nature of water is defined as thinness, flow, and thirst-quenching. Examples are given: ashriba (fruit drinks), khall (vinegar), mā' al-bāqillā' (bean water), and maraq (broth). The commentator also highlights the rhetorical device of 'laf wa nashr' (scattering and gathering) in the original text, showing how the categories of 'squeezed from trees' and 'overcome by another' are intertwined. The example of vinegar is clarified: if mixed with water, it falls under 'overcome'; if pure, it falls under 'squeezed from fruit'. The passage ends with an analogy to a Quranic verse using a similar rhetorical structure. The overall ruling is that such water cannot be used for purification (wudū' or ghusl).

Key Terms
الغلبة (al-ghalaba): Predominance: when a foreign substance overcomes water, either by quantity (parts) or by changing its attributes (color, taste, smell).
طبع الماء (tab' al-mā'): The natural properties of water: thinness (riqqah), fluidity (sayalān), and quenching thirst (taskīn al-'atsh).
لف ونشر (laf wa nashr): Figure of speech where multiple items are mentioned together (laf) and then each is explained separately (nashr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciliation in the passage, which criterion applies to water mixed with milk?
A) By parts (quantity)
B) By attributes (color, taste, smell)
C) By whichever is more predominant
D) It is always permissible regardless of change

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves the intra-madhhab dispute by using parts criterion for same-genus mixtures and attributes criterion for different-genus mixtures.

Water Mixed with Pure Substance: Ruling on Change of Properties
Explains ruling and examples for water mixed with pure substance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water mixed with a pure substance that changes one or two of its properties, and identify the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position and the intra-madhhab disagreement.

Core Idea
Water mixed with pure substance remains purifying unless all three properties change; even two changes do not invalidate according to the sound view.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira expands on al-Quduri's ruling about water mixed with a pure substance. It clarifies that the water's 'nature' (tab')—softness, fluidity, thirst-quenching—may be altered, but the ruling depends on how many of the three properties (taste, color, smell) are changed. The passage also highlights an intra-madhhab dispute on whether water with two changed properties remains valid for ablution, ultimately favoring the permissive view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is using water mixed with a pure substance for purification (taharah). The ruling (hukm) is permissible (ja'iz/mubah) according to the matn.
The ruling is not about impurity but about the water's suitability for wudu.
2
Understand 'tab' and the three properties
'Tab' al-maa' refers to its essential nature: softness, fluidity, quenching thirst. The three 'awsaf' are taste, color, smell. Change of one property does not remove the water from being purifying.
The commentator uses the rhetorical device 'laaf wa nashr' to connect examples.
3
Apply the one-change ruling
If only one property changes (e.g., water becomes reddish from safflower), the water remains purifying and wudu is valid. This is the unanimous position in the passage.
4
Analyze intra-madhhab difference for two changes
If two properties change, the Sheikh (al-Quduri) indicated impermissibility, but the sound (sahih) view permits it. The later authority al-Mustasfa confirms the permissive view.
This is the key ikhtilaf – the relied-upon position is permissibility.
5
Note the special case of three changes from leaves
If all three properties change due to tree leaves in autumn, the majority of Hanafi scholars permit wudu. Al-Maidani, however, says it is drinkable but not valid for wudu.
This shows that even within the school, extreme change may trigger disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

* قوله : (فأخرجه عن طبع الماء) : ي او وطبعه : الرقة والسيلان، وتسكين العطش.
*
قوله : (كالأشربة) : أي المتَّخذة من الثمار، كشراب الرمان. - ثم إن الشيخ راعى في هذا صنعة اللف والنشر، فقوله: اعتصر من الشجر : لَفٌ، وكذا بماء غلب عليه غيره : لَف أيضاً.
وقوله : كالأشربة : تفسير لما اعتصر من الشجر والثمر. وقوله: (والخَل): إن كان المخلوط بالماء: فهو مما غلب عليه غيره، وإن كان خالصاً: فهو مما اعتصر من الثمر.
وقوله : (والمرق) : تفسير لما غَلَبَ عليه غيره.
ونظير هذا قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمِن رَّحْمَتِهِ جَعَلَ لَكُمُ الَّيْلَ وَالنَّهَارَ لِتَسْكُنُوا فِيهِ وَلِتَبْتَغُوا مِن فَضْلِهِ ﴾ القصص / 73.

وماء الزَّرْدَج هو : ماء العُصفر المنقوع، فيُطرح، ولا يُصبغ به.
* قوله: (وتجوز الطهارة بماء خالطه شيء طاهر فغير أحد أوصافه). الأوصاف ثلاثةُ: الطعم واللون والرائحة، فإن غير وصفين: فعلى إشارة الشيخ: لا يجوز الوضوء به، لكن الصحيح أنه يجوز، كذا في «المستصفى».
فإن تغيرت أوصافه الثلاثةُ بوقوع أوراق الأشجار فيه في وقت الخريف: يجوز الوضوء به عند عامة أصحابنا.
وقال الميداني : يجوز شربه ؛ لأنه طاهر، ولا يجوز الوضوء بـه ؛
Thus he removed it from the nature of water, such as beverages, vinegar, bean water, and broth. And if its genus is not the genus of water, like milk, then consideration is given to the descriptions, as Muhammad said, and the Sheikh chose Muhammad's statement, where he said: 'So it changed one of its descriptions.' His statement: 'Thus he removed it from the nature of water': its nature is softness, fluidity, and quenching thirst. His statement: 'such as beverages': those made from fruits, like pomegranate drink. Then the Sheikh observed in this the technique of folding and spreading (laaf wa nashr). His statement: 'pressed from the tree' is folding, and likewise 'or water over which something else has overcome' is also folding. His statement: 'such as beverages': explanation of what is pressed from tree and fruit. His statement: 'and vinegar': if it is mixed with water, it is of the type where something else has overcome it; if it is pure, it is of the type pressed from fruit. His statement: 'and broth': explanation of what something else has overcome. An analogy for this is Allah's statement: 'And out of His mercy He made for you the night and the day that you may rest therein and seek of His bounty' (28:73). His statement: 'that you may rest therein' refers to the night, and 'and seek of His bounty' refers to the day. His statement: 'and bean water': it means cooked, such that when it cools it thickens. If it is not cooked, it is of the type: 'And purification is permissible with water mixed with a pure substance.' Bean is 'fool' (broad beans); if you double the lam you shorten it, if you lighten it you lengthen it; the singular is 'baqla'ah' or 'baqlaa'ah' with tashdid or takhfif. His statement: 'and safflower water'. He mentioned it as a counterpart of broth, and the correct view is that it is a type of: 'And purification is permissible with water mixed with a pure substance.' Safflower water is the water in which safflower has been soaked; it is thrown away, not used for dyeing. His statement: 'And purification is permissible with water mixed with a pure substance that changes one of its three descriptions.' The descriptions are three: taste, color, and smell. If it changes two descriptions: according to the Sheikh's indication, it is not permissible to perform ablution with it, but the correct view is that it is permissible, as in 'al-Mustasfa'. If all three descriptions change due to falling tree leaves in it during autumn, ablution with it is permissible according to the generality of our companions. Al-Maidani said: It is permissible to drink it because it is pure, but it is not permissible to perform ablution with it.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling from Mukhtasar al-Quduri on water mixed with a pure substance. The commentator begins by explaining that the matn's expression 'Thus he removed it from the nature of water' refers to water that has been altered by a pure substance, removing it from its original nature of softness, fluidity, and quenching thirst. Examples include beverages made from fruits, vinegar, cooked bean water (which thickens upon cooling), broth, and safflower water (soaked safflower water). The commentator notes that the Sheikh (al-Quduri) employs the rhetorical device of laaf wa nashr (folding and spreading) in listing these examples: some are pressed from trees/fruits, others are substances that have overcome water. The same device appears in the Qur'anic verse about night and day. The core ruling is then restated: purification is permissible with water mixed with a pure substance that changes one of its three descriptions (taste, color, smell). If two descriptions change, the Sheikh's view (apparently referring to a previous position) does not permit ablution, but the sound (sahih) view, as in al-Mustasfa, permits it. If all three change due to tree leaves in autumn, the majority of Hanafi scholars permit ablution. Al-Maidani (a Hanafi scholar) holds that such water is pure and drinkable but not valid for ablution, indicating an intra-madhhab divergence. The passage clarifies that the ruling depends on the nature of the substance (pure vs. impure) and the extent of change.

Key Terms
طبع الماء (tab' al-maa'): The natural properties of water: softness, fluidity, and quenching thirst.
الأوصاف (al-awsaf): The three qualities of water: taste, color, and smell.
صنعة اللف والنشر (sana'at al-laaf wa al-nashr): A rhetorical device where items are mentioned in a folded manner (laaf) and then unfolded (nashr) with their corresponding explanations.
الصحيح (al-sahih): The correct or sound view in the Hanafi school, often the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is not permissible according to all Hanafis.
B) It is permissible according to the majority, but al-Maidani permits drinking only.
C) It is permissible only if the leaves are not toxic.
D) It is not permissible because all three properties changed.

Key Takeaway
Water mixed with a pure substance remains valid for purification if one property changes; even with two or three changes, the sound Hanafi view permits wudu, though some authorities differ.

Ruling on Water Mixed with Pure Substances for Purification
Explaining the permissibility of using water mixed with pure substances for wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissibility of wudu with water mixed with pure substances, and explain the internal dispute between al-Maidani and the majority.

Core Idea
Water changed by a pure substance remains valid for wudu if still called water — even if all three properties alter.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayra expands on al-Quduri's matn regarding water mixed with pure substances. It clarifies that the water remains pure (tahir) and suitable for wudu, despite changes in taste, color, or smell, as long as the substance is pure and the water retains its name. The text also addresses an internal Hanafi difference of opinion led by al-Maidani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three properties of water
The passage states that the properties (awṣāf) of water are taste, color, and smell. Change in one or more of these is the criterion discussed.
These are the standard Hanafi criteria.
2
State the general ruling from the matn
The base ruling is that purification is permissible with water that has been mixed with a pure substance, even if one property changes.
The matn is rule-based.
3
Note the intra-madhhab difference
Al-Maidani held that water changed in properties is not valid for wudu, while the majority (عامة أصحابنا) permit it. The text declares the majority view as correct (as-sahih).
The disagreement is over the status of changed water as absolute water (mā' mutlaq).
4
Understand the mufta bihi and its basis
The relied-upon position is that wudu is valid even if all three properties change, as long as the mixture is with a pure substance and the water remains called 'water'.
Examples include flood water, soap, saffron, and alum mixtures.
5
Apply the ruling to everyday scenarios
Recognize that common situations like water colored by leaves or treated with pure chemicals are permissible for wudu.
The principle is that minimal or unavoidable mixing with pure substances does not invalidate the water for purification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And purification is permissible with water that something pure has mixed with and changed one of its properties. If its three properties change due to the fall of tree leaves in it during autumn time: it is permissible to do wudu with it according to the general body of our companions. (1) And al-Maidani said: It is permissible to drink it because it is pure, but it is not permissible to do wudu with it. (1) And likewise if alum mixes with water until it blackens: it is upon this.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the matn's ruling regarding water mixed with pure substances. The matn states that purification is permissible with water that a pure substance has mixed with and changed one of its three properties (taste, color, smell). The sharh begins by explaining the linguistic note on 'باقلاء' (fava beans), then proceeds to discuss 'ماء الزردج' (safflower water), which is considered a sub-case of the general ruling. Then the commentator restates the matn and explains that if two properties change, the Sheikh (a reference to a Hanafi authority) ruled it impermissible, but the correct view (as-sahih) is that it is permissible, as mentioned in al-Mustasfā. If all three properties change due to leaves falling in autumn, it is permissible according to the majority of our companions (عامة أصحابنا). A difference is noted: al-Maidani held that such water is permissible to drink but not for wudu. The commentator then discusses examples: flood water (ماء المد) which carries scum and leaves, water mixed with potash, soap, and saffron — all are permissible because the name 'water' remains and a small amount of these substances cannot be avoided. Similarly, water mixed with alum turning black is also included. The overarching principle is that as long as the water remains called 'water' and the mixture is unavoidable or minimal, it is valid for wudu.

Key Terms
أوصاف الماء (awṣāf al-mā'): The three properties of water: taste (طعم), color (لون), and smell (رائحة).
ماء الزردج (mā' al-zardaj): Water in which safflower (العصفر) has been soaked, then discarded; not used for dyeing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits wudu with water mixed with pure substances as long as it remains called 'water', even if all three properties change, following the majority opinion.

Water Contaminated by Impurity: Hanafi Ruling
Stating the prohibition of using water contaminated with impurity for purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on water contaminated by impurity and apply it to distinguish between flowing water, still water, and large ponds.

Core Idea
Non-flowing water becomes impure when any impurity falls in; for a large pond, only the spot of contamination is impure.

Understanding when water becomes impure (najis) is essential for valid purification (wudu and ghusl). This lesson explains the Hanafi position on water into which impurity falls, clarifying the important distinction between small containers and large ponds. The ruling also applies when we strongly suspect impurity has entered the water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is using water for wudu (purification). The ruling: if the water has been contaminated by impurity, it cannot be used for wudu; doing so is prohibited (haram).
The basic ruling is unconditional: any impurity in water renders it unfit.
2
Distinguish between flowing and still water
The passage specifies that this ruling applies to non-flowing water (ghayr jari) such as wells, containers, and ponds. Flowing water (jari) does not become impure from a small impurity; the flow carries it away.
The Hanafi school considers the flow as a purifying factor.
3
Understand the nuance for large ponds
For a large pond (ghadir 'azim), impurity only contaminates the spot where it fell, not the entire body of water. The rest remains pure and usable.
A large pond is estimated as 10x10 cubits (approx. 5x5 meters).
4
Apply the rule to strong suspicion
If one strongly suspects (ghalabat al-zann) that impurity has entered the water, the same ruling applies: the water is considered impure.
This is based on the principle that certainty is not removed by doubt, but strong probability acts like certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وكل ماء وقعت فيه نجاسة: لم يجز الوضوء به). وكذا إذا غلب على ظننا ذلك. وأراد به غير الجاري، وما هو في معناه، كالغدير العظيم. قوله: (قليلاً) كان الماء أو كثيراً) أي قليلاً، كالآبار والأواني، أو كثيراً، كالغدير: فينجس موضع الوقوع وإن كان كثيراً.
His statement: (And every water into which impurity falls: it is not permissible to perform wudu with it). And likewise if our assumption predominates that. And he meant by it non-flowing water, and what is in its meaning, like the great pond. His statement: (Little) whether the water is little or much, meaning little like wells and vessels, or much like a pond: then the place of falling becomes impure even if it is much.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling on water that becomes contaminated with impurity (najasa). The matn states unconditionally that such water cannot be used for wudu. The commentary specifies that this ruling applies to non-flowing water (غير الجاري), such as still ponds. For flowing water, impurity does not render the water impure as long as the flow continues. The text further explains that regardless of whether the quantity of water is small (like wells or small containers) or large (like a big pond), the impurity contaminates the water. However, for a large pond, only the immediate spot where the impurity fell becomes impure, not the entire pond. This is an important nuance: the water is considered impure only at the point of contact, leaving the rest of the pond pure. The statement 'وكذا إذا غلب على ظننا ذلك' indicates that even if we are not certain but strongly suspect that impurity entered, the same ruling applies.

Key Terms
نجاسة (najāsah): Ritual impurity that, when it falls into water, renders it impure (najis) for purification purposes.
ماء غير جاري (mā' ghayr jārī): Non-flowing water, such as water in wells, ponds, and containers.
غدير عظيم (ghadīr 'aẓīm): A large pond or body of standing water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling on using water from a large pond (غدير عظيم) into which a small amount of impurity has fallen?
A) The entire pond becomes impure and cannot be used for wudu.
B) Only the spot where the impurity fell becomes impure; the rest remains pure.
C) The water remains pure if the impurity is small in quantity.
D) The pond water can be used only for drinking, not for wudu.

Key Takeaway
Non-flowing water becomes impure when any impurity falls in, but for a large pond, only the spot of falling becomes impure.
