The Ruling and Internal Dispute on Used Water
Commentary on used water ruling with internal Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi positions on used water, explain the definitional dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and state the mufta bihi ruling with its practical effect.

Core Idea
Used water is pure but not purifying: three internal Hanafi positions, one operative ruling.

Water that has been used for ritual purification cannot be reused for the same purpose. But what is its exact legal status? The Hanafi school reports three different transmissions from Imam Abu Hanifa, ranging from severe impurity to pure but non-purifying. This lesson unpacks those positions, the reasoning behind them, and the relied-upon view that balances textual evidence with practical ease. You will also learn the precise definition of musta'mal water and how intention affects its classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Start by reading the opening ruling: used water is not permissible for hadath removal. Note the restriction to hadath, implying it may still remove physical impurities.
This is the undisputed starting point.
2
Map the three internal transmissions
List the three positions from Abu Hanifa: (1) severely impure (Hasan), (2) lightly impure (Abu Yusuf), (3) pure but non-purifying (Muhammad). Identify the practical consequence of each.
Understand the spectrum from najis to tahir.
3
Analyze the tarjih (preference)
See why the commentator favors Muhammad's view: practicality (garments cannot be protected from small amounts) and authority (scholars of Iraq). Understand that this is the mufta bihi.
The tarjih is based on ease and scholarly consensus.
4
Define musta'mal according to Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Compare the two definitions: Abu Yusuf says any water that removes hadath or is used on the body with devotion; Muhammad requires intention of devotion for both. Derive the four scenarios to see when water becomes musta'mal by consensus.
The fourth scenario is cut off in the source but can be inferred.
5
Apply to a practical case
Consider a scenario: water used for wudu by a person who is pure and intends cooling. According to the mufta bihi, is it musta'mal? According to Muhammad, no (no intention of qurba). According to Abu Yusuf, yes (it removes hadath by a pure person? Actually the passage says for a pure person, intention is needed per Abu Yusuf too? Let's clarify: the passage says for a pure person, intention makes it musta'mal by consensus; without intention, it does not become musta'mal by consensus. So in this scenario, without intention, it is not musta'mal. So the water remains pure and purifying.
This shows the practical importance of the definition.

غَسَلَ أعضاءه من وَسَخ أو تراب، وهو في هذا كله محدث. وقوله : على وجه القُربة : بأن توضأ وهو طاهر بنية الطهارة.
ويتفرع من هذا أربع مسائل:

And used water: it is not permissible to use it in purification from hadath. He restricted it to hadath because it removes impurities. And it is the same whether one performs wudu with it or performs ghusl from janaba: it is used. And it is disliked to drink it. And there is disagreement about its description: al-Hasan reported from Abu Hanifa that it is impure with severe impurity, to the extent that if more than a dirham's amount touches the garment, it prevents prayer. And this is very far-fetched, because garments cannot be preserved from a small amount of it, and it cannot be avoided. (1) Mughni al-Muhtaj 78/1. And 'musta'mal' is: every water by which a hadath is removed, or is used on the body in the manner of devotion. And Abu Yusuf reported from Abu Hanifa: it is impure with light impurity, like the urine of animals whose meat is eaten, and this is what the scholars of Balkh adopted. And Muhammad reported from Abu Hanifa: it is pure but not purifying for hadath, like vinegar and milk, and this is the correct view, and the scholars of Iraq adopted it. And it is the same whether the person performing wudu was pure or had hadath in its being musta'mal. * His statement: (And musta'mal: every water by which hadath is removed, or used on the body in the manner of devotion). This is the statement of Abu Yusuf, and it is said it is also the statement of Abu Hanifa. And Muhammad said: it does not become musta'mal except with the intention of devotion. So his statement: 'removed by it hadath' is when one performs wudu for cooling, or teaches someone wudu, or washes his limbs from dirt or dust, and he is in all this in a state of hadath. And his statement: 'in the manner of devotion' is when one performs wudu while pure with the intention of purification. And from this arise four issues: 1 - If a person with hadath performs wudu and intends devotion: it becomes musta'mal by consensus. 2 - If a pure person performs wudu and does not intend it: it does not become musta'mal by consensus. 2- [should be 3] - If a pure person performs wudu and intends it: it becomes musta'mal by consensus; because according to Abu...
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on used water (الْماء المستعمل). The basic ruling is that it is not permissible to use used water for removing ritual impurity (hadath). The commentator notes that this restriction applies only to hadath, because used water can still remove physical impurities (najas). He clarifies that whether one performs wudu or ghusl with it, it is considered used. It is disliked (makruh) to drink such water. Then the passage delves into the internal Hanafi dispute regarding the legal status of used water. Three transmissions from Imam Abu Hanifa are reported: (1) Through al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: it is severely impure (najis najasa ghaliza). (2) Through Abu Yusuf: it is lightly impure (najis najasa mukhaffafa), like the urine of animals whose meat is eaten. (3) Through Muhammad al-Shaybani: it is pure but not purifying (tahir ghayr mutahhir), like vinegar or milk. The commentator states that the third view is the correct one (as-sahih) and is adopted by the scholars of Iraq. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position. The passage then defines 'musta'mal' according to Abu Yusuf: any water that has been used to remove hadath or has been used on the body with the intention of devotion (qurba). Muhammad restricts it further, requiring intention. Four scenarios are derived, though the text cuts off part of the fourth. This definition clarifies when water becomes musta'mal: if a person with hadath uses water with or without intention, it becomes musta'mal by consensus (except Muhammad's requirement of intention for the pure person).

Key Terms
الماء المستعمل (al-mā' al-musta'mal): Water that has been used for removing ritual impurity (hadath) or used on the body with the intention of devotion (qurba), according to the Hanafi school.
طاهر غير مطهر (tāhir ghayr muṭahhir): A category of water: it is pure (tahir) in itself but not legally purifying for removing hadath or najas. It cannot be used for wudu or ghusl but does not itself impart impurity.
نجاسة غليظة (najāsa ghalīẓa): Severe impurity; according to al-Hasan's transmission, used water is severely impure, comparable to urine and feces, requiring washing with water if it falls on clothes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school, used water is:
A) Najis najasa ghaliza
B) Najis najasa mukhaffafa
C) Tahir ghayr mutahhir
D) Mutahhir (pure and purifying)

Key Takeaway
Used water in the Hanafi school is pure but not purifying; the mufta bihi is Muhammad's transmission, and intention of devotion is key to defining when water becomes musta'mal.

Musta'mal Water: Definition, Intra-Hanafi Dispute, and Ruling
Defines musta'mal water and explains intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi definition of musta'mal water, distinguish the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on its purity, and apply the definition to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Musta'mal water is tahir (pure) but not mutahhir (purifying) – it can be used but not for ritual purification.

Water used in wudu or ghusl takes on a new legal character. But does it become impure? Or merely lose its purifying power? In this passage, the author of al-Jawhara al-Nayyira clarifies the definition of musta'mal water and the internal Hanafi disagreement on its ruling. The relied-upon view is that such water remains pure but cannot be used again for wudu or ghusl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition from the matn
Read the opening line: 'And used water is: every water by which an impurity is removed, or that is used on the body for the purpose of devotion.' Note the two conditions: removing hadath or qurbah.
This definition is attributed to Abu Yusuf and also to Abu Hanifa.
2
Note the two narrations from Abu Hanifa
There are two narrations: (1) via Abu Yusuf: musta'mal is najis mukhaffafa; (2) via Muhammad: musta'mal is tahir ghair mutahhir. The latter is considered sahih and adopted by Iraqi scholars.
The Balkh scholars followed Abu Yusuf's narration.
3
Understand the four derived cases
The text branches into four scenarios combining tahir/muhdith and presence/absence of qurbah intention. Identify which are agreed and which is disputed (the muhdith without intention).
This clarifies exactly when water becomes musta'mal.
4
Examine the hadith proof for qurbah
The hadith 'Wudu before food removes poverty...' is cited to show that washing hands for food is an act of devotion, making the water musta'mal.
This is a dalil naqli supporting the qurbah category.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its implication
The author declares Muhammad's narration as correct and adopted by Iraqi scholars. Thus the operative ruling: musta'mal is tahir ghair mutahhir.
Practical consequence: musta'mal water can be used for cooking or cleaning but not for wudu or ghusl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمستعمَلُ : كلُّ ماءٍ أُزيل به حَدَثُ، أو استعمل في البدن على وجـه القربة .
وروى أبو يوسف عن أبي حنيفة : أنه نجس نجاسة مخففة، كبول ما يؤكل لحمه ، وبهذا أخذ مشايخ بلخ.
وروى محمد عن أبي حنيفة : أنه طاهر غير مطهر للأحداث، كالخل واللبن، وهذا هو الصحيح، وبه أخذ مشايخ العراق.
وسواء في ذلك كان المتوضئ طاهراً أو محدثاً في كونه مستعملاً.
قوله: (والمستعمل : كلُّ ماء أزيل به حدث، أو استعمل في البدن على وجه القربة). هذا قول أبي يوسف، وقيل: هو قول أبي حنيفة أيضاً.
وقال محمد : لا مستعمل إلا بنية القربة.
فيتفرع من هذا أربع مسائل:
١ - إذا توضأ المحدث ونوى القُربة: صار مستعملاً، إجماعاً.
٢ - وإذا توضأ الطاهر ولم ينوها : لا يصير مستعملاً، إجماعاً.
٣ - وإذا توضأ الطاهر ونواها صار مستعملاً، إجماعاً؛ لأن عند أبي يوسف يصير مستعملاً بأحد شرطين : إما أن يستعمله بنية القربة، أو يرفع به الحدث.
٤ - والرابعة : وهي مسألة الخلاف، وهي: ما إذا توضأ المحدث ولم ينوها : فعند أبي يوسف: يكون مستعملاً، وعند محمد : لا يصير مستعملاً. ولو كان جنباً واغتسل للتبرد : صار مستعملاً عند أبي يوسف، خلافاً لمحمد.
وقوله : في البدن: قيد به؛ لأن ما كان من غسالة الجمادات ، كالقدور والقصاع والحجارة: لا يكون مستعملاً.
وكذا إذا غسل ثوباً من الوسخ من غير نجاسة: لا يكون مستعملاً. وإن غسل يده للطعام أو من الطعام كان مستعملاً؛ لأنه تقرب، قال عليه الصلاة السلام : الوضوء قبل الطعام ينفي الفقر، وبعده: ينفي اللمم، يعني: الجنون.
(والحديث عند ابن ماجه وغيره بلفظ: «مَن أحب أن يكثر الله خير بيته فليتوضأ إذا حضر غداؤه، وإذا رفع»)
And used water is: every water by which an impurity is removed, or that is used on the body for the purpose of devotion. And Abu Yusuf narrated from Abu Hanifa: that it is impure with a light impurity, like the urine of an animal whose meat is eaten, and this is what the mashayikh of Balkh adopted. And Muhammad narrated from Abu Hanifa: that it is pure but not purifying for ritual impurities, like vinegar and milk, and this is the correct view, and it is what the mashayikh of Iraq adopted. And it is equal in that whether the person performing ablution was pure or in a state of impurity regarding its being used. His saying: (And used water is: every water by which an impurity is removed, or that is used on the body for the purpose of devotion). This is the saying of Abu Yusuf, and it is said that it is also the saying of Abu Hanifa. And Muhammad said: There is no used water except with the intention of devotion. From this branch four issues: 1. If a person in a state of impurity performs ablution and intends devotion: it becomes used water, by consensus. 2. If a pure person performs ablution and does not intend it: it does not become used water, by consensus. 3. If a pure person performs ablution and intends it: it becomes used water, by consensus; because according to Abu Yusuf it becomes used water by one of two conditions: either he uses it with the intention of devotion, or he removes impurity with it. 4. The fourth, and it is the issue of disagreement, is: if a person in a state of impurity performs ablution and does not intend it: according to Abu Yusuf, it becomes used water, and according to Muhammad, it does not become used water. And if a person in major impurity bathed for cooling off: it becomes used water according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. And his saying: 'on the body' is a restriction; because that which is from the washing of inanimate objects, like pots, bowls, and stones, does not become used water. Similarly, if he washes a garment from dirt without impurity: it does not become used water. And if he washes his hand for food or from food, it becomes used water because it is devotion. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Wudu before food removes poverty, and after it removes al-lamam, meaning madness.'
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition of musta'mal (used water) from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The author begins by quoting the matn that defines musta'mal as water used to remove hadath (ritual impurity) or used on the body for qurbah (devotion). He then notes the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: according to a narration by Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, such water is najis mukhaffafa (lightly impure), similar to the urine of a halal meat animal. This was the position of the Balkh scholars. On the other hand, a narration by Muhammad al-Shaybani from Abu Hanifa holds that it is tahir (pure) but not mutahhir (purifying) for hadath, like vinegar or milk. This is deemed sahih (correct) and adopted by the Iraqi scholars, and it is the mufta bihi. The author then clarifies that the definition applies whether the user was tahir or muhdith. He attributes the definition to Abu Yusuf, though some say it is also Abu Hanifa's view. Muhammad restricts musta'mal to usage with the intention of qurbah. From this, four cases branch out: (1) a muhdith intending qurbah: musta'mal by consensus; (2) a tahir without intention: not musta'mal by consensus; (3) a tahir with intention: musta'mal by consensus; (4) a muhdith without intention: disputed – Abu Yusuf says musta'mal (since hadath is removed), Muhammad says not musta'mal (since no qurbah intention). Similarly, a junub bathing for cooling off becomes musta'mal according to Abu Yusuf but not according to Muhammad. The restriction 'on the body' excludes washing inanimate objects like pots, so such water is not musta'mal. Washing hands for food is considered qurbah, supported by a hadith about wudu before and after food removing poverty and madness. The hadith is cited as evidence for the devotional nature of wudu for food, thus making that water musta'mal.

Key Terms
مستعمل (musta'mal): Water that has been used either to remove ritual impurity (hadath) or on the body with the intention of devotion (qurbah).
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity, either minor (hadath asghar) or major (hadath akbar) that requires wudu or ghusl to remove.
قربة (qurbah): An act of devotion that brings one closer to Allah, such as wudu before eating, which is considered a sunnah act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, what is the ruling on water that has been used to remove hadath without the intention of qurbah?
A) It is najis mukhaffafa and cannot be used at all.
B) It is tahir (pure) but not mutahhir (purifying) for ritual purification.
C) It remains tahir and mutahhir.
D) It becomes makruh but still valid for wudu.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view: musta'mal water is pure (tahir) but not purifying (ghair mutahhir) for ritual use, and it becomes musta'mal by removing hadath or by use with qurbah intention.

Conditions for Water Becoming Musta'mal and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on musta'mal conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions that make water musta'mal according to Abu Yusuf and explain Muhammad's objection.

Core Idea
Water becomes musta'mal by intent of worship or removal of hadath, but the role of intent is disputed within the Hanafi school.

This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayrah explains when water used for purification becomes musta'mal (used) and thus no longer suitable for further ablution. The author presents two general conditions—intent of worship (qurbah) or removal of hadath—and then highlights an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the necessity of intent. The passage also clarifies that washing inanimate objects or cleaning clothes from dirt does not render water musta'mal, but washing hands for food does, supported by a hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for musta'mal water
The passage states that water becomes musta'mal if used with intent of worship (qurbah) or if it removes hadath. Note that these are alternatives.
The condition of cooling is introduced later in the dispute.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that water becomes musta'mal even without specific intent (e.g., bathing for cooling), while Muhammad requires intent of qurbah or removal of hadath. Note the thamarah al-khilaf: cooling bath water becomes musta'mal only according to Abu Yusuf.
3
Distinguish cases where water does NOT become musta'mal
Washing inanimate objects (pots, stones) or cleaning clothes from dirt (without impurity) does not make water musta'mal. Only washing for food is considered qurbah.
4
Analyze the hadith citation
The hadith about wudu before/after food is cited to support that washing hands for food is an act of worship, making water musta'mal. The footnote discusses its authenticity (hasan) and the permissibility of acting on weak hadith in virtues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوسف يصير مستعملاً بأحد شرطين : إما أن يستعمله بنية القربة، أو يرفع به الحدث.
٤ـ والرابعة : وهي ينوها : فعند أبي يوسف: يكون مستعملاً، ، وعند محمد : لا مسألة الخلاف، وهي: : ما إذا توضأ المحدث ولم مستعملاً. يصير ولو كان جنباً واغتسل للتبرد : صار مستعملاً عند أبي يوسف، خلافاً لمحمد.
وقوله : في البدن: قيد به؛ لأن ما كان من غسالة الجمادات ، كالقدور والقصاع والحجارة: لا يكون مستعملاً.
وكذا إذا غسل ثوباً من الوسخ من غير نجاسة: لا يكون مستعملاً. وإن غسل يده للطعام أو من الطعام كان مستعملاً؛ لأنه تقرب، قال عليه الصلاة السلام : الوضوء قبل الطعام ينفي الفقر، وبعده: ينفي اللمم ()، يعني: الجنون.
(۱) مسند الشهاب ٢٠٥/١، المعجم الأوسط للطبراني ١٦٤/٧، وبلفظ: «مَن أحب أن يكثر الله خير بيته فليتوضأ إذا حضر غداؤه، وإذا رفع»: عند ابن ماجه (٣٢٦٠)، وبلفظ : «الوضوء قبل الطعام وبعده بركة الطعام عند الحاكم في المستدرك ١٠٦/٤ ، وغيره من كتب السنن، كأبي داود (٣٧٥٥)، والترمذي (٣٦١٤)، وينظر مجمع الزوائد ٢٣/٥ ، وفيض القدير للمناوي ٣٧٦/٦، وإتحاف السادة المتقين ٢١٢/٥، وفي كل طريق لوحده ضعف وكلام، وبهذه الطرق المتعددة، والشواهد المتتابعة يكسب الحديث فضل قوة، بل لا يخرج إسناده عن حد الحسن، كما قال المنذري في الترغيب والترهيب ١٥٠/٣ ، وأيضاً فعلى القول بضعفه: فإنه يستحب العمل بالضعيف في فضائل الأعمال، ولا شك أن النظافة وغسل اليدين منها، والله أعلم.
Yusuf becomes musta'mal with one of two conditions: either he uses it with the intention of drawing near, or he removes hadath with it. 4 - The fourth: and it is what they intend: according to Abu Yusuf: it becomes musta'mal, and according to Muhammad: no, the point of difference, namely: when a person with hadath performs wudu and does not become musta'mal. Even if he was junub and bathed for cooling: it becomes musta'mal according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. And his saying: 'in the body' is a restriction; because what comes from the washing of inanimate objects, like pots, bowls, and stones, is not musta'mal. Similarly, if he washes a garment from dirt without impurity, it is not musta'mal. But if he washes his hand for food or from food, it becomes musta'mal because it is an act of drawing near. He (peace be upon him) said: 'Wudu before food removes poverty, and after it removes lack [meaning insanity].' (1) Musnad al-Shihab 1/205, al-Mu'jam al-Awsat lil-Tabarani 7/164, with the wording: 'Whoever wishes that Allah increase good in his house should perform wudu when his lunch is present, and when it is lifted' in Ibn Majah (3260), and with the wording: 'Wudu before and after food is a blessing of food' in al-Hakim (4/106), and other books of Sunan, like Abu Dawud (3755), al-Tirmidhi (3614), and see Majma' al-Zawa'id 5/23, Fayd al-Qadir 6/376, Ithaf al-Sada al-Muttaqin 5/212, and each path alone has weakness and criticism, but with these multiple paths and successive witnesses, the hadith gains strength, rather its chain does not fall below the level of hasan, as al-Mundhiri said in al-Targhib wa al-Tarhib 3/150. Also, even if it is weak, it is recommended to act upon a weak hadith in virtues of deeds, and there is no doubt that cleanliness and washing hands are among them, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage discusses when water becomes 'musta'mal' (used) in Hanafi fiqh, meaning water that has been used for purification and is no longer pure for further use. The author states two conditions: intent of worship (qurbah) or removal of hadath (ritual impurity). Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Abu Yusuf holds that water becomes musta'mal even if the user had no specific intent (e.g., bathing for cooling), while Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees. The text also clarifies that washing inanimate objects or cleaning clothes from dirt (without impurity) does not make water musta'mal. However, washing hands for food is considered an act of worship, so water becomes musta'mal, supported by a hadith on wudu before and after meals. The footnote discusses the hadith's authenticity, concluding it is hasan and acceptable for virtuous deeds.

Key Terms
مستعمل (musta'mal): Water that has been used for washing or purification and is no longer pure for further ablution (though it is not najis).
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity that prevents salah; minor (hadath asghar) removed by wudu, major (hadath akbar) removed by ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following makes water musta'mal?
A) Washing a pot with water
B) Washing hands before eating food
C) Washing a garment with water to remove dirt
D) Washing stones with water

Key Takeaway
Water becomes musta'mal only when used for qurbah or removal of hadath; the role of intent is disputed between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Purification of Hides by Tanning and Slaughter
Hadith-based ruling with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on meat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification of hides by tanning and slaughter, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the meat.

Core Idea
Tanning purifies any hide; slaughter also purifies hide and meat according to al-Hidayah, but later authorities disagree.

This lesson examines the classical Hanafi ruling that tanning purifies animal hides, and the extension that legal slaughter (dhabh) also purifies hide and meat. We will explore the supporting hadith, the general rule equating tanning and slaughter, the intra-Hanafi dispute about the meat, the conditions for valid slaughter, and the exception for snakes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its evidence
The passage begins with the hadith 'Every hide that is tanned is purified.' Recognize this as the primary dalil for tanning's purifying effect. Note that 'ihab' refers to untanned skin; after tanning it becomes 'adim'.
This is a general ruling covering all hides except those that cannot withstand tanning.
2
Understand the general rule equating tanning and slaughter
The text states: 'Every skin that is purified by tanning is purified by slaughter, and that which is not (is not).' This means that the methods are interchangeable for purification, except for intrinsic exceptions like snakes.
The rule links purification by tanning to purification by slaughter by way of analogy (qiyas).
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute on meat
Three positions are cited: al-Hidayah holds that slaughter purifies both hide and meat even for non-edible animals; al-Fatawa says meat is not purified; al-Nihayah limits purification to animals that are not najis al-ayn (intrinsically impure).
This dispute only concerns meat; the hide is purified according to all views if conditions are met.
4
Note the conditions for slaughter to purify
For slaughter to purify, the slaughterer must be from 'ahl al-dhakah bi al-tasmiyah' (Muslim or People of the Book who say Bismillah). A Magian's slaughter is invalid, so the hide needs tanning. Also, the cut must be between the collarbone and jaw.
These conditions ensure the slaughter is legally valid (dhabh shar'i).
5
Apply the exception for snakes
The snake's shed slough (qamis al-hayyah) is pure, but its actual skin is impure and cannot be purified by tanning because it cannot bear the process.
This is an exception to the general rule; snakes are considered intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn) like pigs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قيل : للطعام يصير مستعملاً، ومنه : لا قوله: (وكلُّ إهاب دبغ فقد طهر).
الإهاب: الجلد الذي لم يُدبَغ، فإذا دبغ : سُمِّي أديماً.
وكل جلد يطهر بالدباغ : فإنه يطهر بالذكاة، وما لا فلا. - وفي «الهداية ) : ما طَهُر بالدباغ: طهر بالذكاة، وكذا لحمه، في الصحيح وإن لم يكن مأكولاً.

(۲) وهو الذي تبدله الحية كل مدة من الزمن، ويكون فوق جلدها.
And every hide that is tanned is purified. It was said: for food it becomes musta'mal, and from that: not his saying: 'Every hide that is tanned is purified.' The ihab: the skin that has not been tanned; when it is tanned, it is called adam. And every skin that is purified by tanning: it is purified by slaughter, and that which is not (is not). And in al-Hidayah: What is purified by tanning is purified by slaughter, and similarly its meat, in the correct view, even if it is not edible. And in al-Fatawa: the correct view is that its meat is not purified. And in al-Nihayah: its meat is only purified if it is not impure in residual liquid. Then according to the statement of the author of al-Hidayah: its meat and skin are purified by slaughter only if the slaughter meets legal conditions: the slaughterer is from the people of slaughter with tasmiyah; but if he is a Magian, then tanning is necessary for the skin because his act is a trust, not slaughter. It is also required that the slaughter be at its proper place, which is between the collarbone and the jaw. And the snake's slough (2): pure, so said al-Hilwani, and its skin: impure, not purified by tanning because it cannot bear it. (2) Which is what the snake sheds every period of time, and is above its skin.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage addresses the purification of animal hides and meat. It begins with the well-known hadith-based ruling: 'Every hide that is tanned is purified.' The author clarifies that 'ihab' means untanned skin; after tanning it becomes 'adim' (leather). A general rule: any skin that is purified by tanning is also purified by legal slaughter (dhabh), and vice versa. The author then reports an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to al-Hidayah, slaughter purifies both the skin and the meat, even if the animal is not edible (e.g., wolf). However, al-Fatawa holds that meat is not purified by slaughter. Al-Nihayah restricts purification of meat to cases where the animal is not intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn). The passage then qualifies: for meat and skin to be purified by slaughter, the slaughter must be legally valid—the slaughterer must be from the People of the Book or a Muslim who says Bismillah. A Magian's slaughter is not valid, so the skin still requires tanning. Also, the cut must be at the proper place. Finally, the snake's slough (the outer skin shed by snakes) is pure, but the snake's actual skin is impure and cannot be purified by tanning because it cannot withstand the process.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihab): The skin of an animal before tanning; after tanning it is called 'adim'.
دباغ (dibagh): The process of treating animal skin with tanning agents to purify it and make it usable.
ذكاة (dhabh / dhakah): Legal slaughter of an animal for food, which can also purify the hide and meat according to some Hanafi opinions.
قميص الحية (qamis al-hayyah): The slough or outer skin shed by a snake; ruled as pure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The animal must be a sheep or goat

Key Takeaway
Tanning purifies any hide that can bear it; valid slaughter also purifies hide and, according to the dominant Hanafi view, the meat as well, but with an intra-madhhab dispute.

The Purification of Hides by Tanning
Defines ihab and states its purification by tanning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'ihab' and state the Hanafi ruling that tanning makes it pure (tahir).

Core Idea
Tanning transforms the impure hide into pure leather.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational ruling from the Book of Purification: that every tanned hide becomes pure. The passage defines the key term 'ihab' (untanned skin) and explains that after tanning it is called 'adim' (leather). Understanding this ruling is essential for knowing which animal skins can be used for prayer mats, water containers, and other purposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the ruling
The passage begins with 'وكل إهاب دبغ' – 'every hide that is tanned'. First, understand what 'إهاب' means. It is defined as the untanned animal skin.
After tanning, it is no longer called ihab but adim.
2
State the ruling
The matn says 'فقد طهر' – 'then it has become pure'. This is a clear statement that tanning purifies the hide regardless of its previous state of najasah (ritual impurity).
3
Note the terminology shift
The commentator clarifies: 'الإهاب: الجلد الذي لم يُدبَغ، فإذا دبغ سمي أديماً'. The ihab is the skin not yet tanned; once tanned, it is called adim (leather). This terminological distinction is important for fiqh rulings.
4
Consider the alternative opinion mentioned
The passage briefly alludes to an alternative view that the tanned hide becomes a 'used utensil' for food, but this is not the relied-upon position. The commentator signals this with 'وقيل' (and it is said) and then rejects it.
The relied-upon view is that the hide becomes pure, not merely 'used'.
5
Conclude the legal status
The final ruling: any animal hide (except pig and human, by other texts) becomes pure and permissible to use once it is tanned. This is the established Hanafi position.
Tanning removes the ritually impure moisture and changes the substance, making it tahir.

And it is said: for food it becomes used [utensil], and from it: no, his saying: 'And every hide that is tanned has become pure.' It becomes used. The 'ihab' is the skin that has not been tanned. When it is tanned, it is called 'adim' (leather).

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn: any untanned hide (إهاب) becomes pure (طاهر) through the process of tanning (دبغ). The commentator defines 'إهاب' as the skin that hasn't been tanned, and after tanning it is called 'أديم'. He also mentions an alternative opinion that the hide becomes a used utensil for food, but that is not the relied-upon view.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihāb): Untanned animal skin
دبغ (dabgh): The process of tanning that purifies the hide
أديم (adīm): Leather, i.e., a tanned hide

Purification by Slaughter and Tanning: Interchangeability and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains interchangeability, intra-Hanafi dispute, and conditions for purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for purification of hides and meat by slaughter and tanning, and identify the points of disagreement among Hanafi scholars regarding meat purification.

Core Idea
Tanning and slaughter are interchangeable for purifying hides, but purification of meat by slaughter is disputed within the Hanafi school.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explores the relationship between two methods of purification: slaughter (dhakāt) and tanning (dabgh). While the general rule is that any hide purified by tanning is also purified by slaughter, there is a notable intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether the meat is also purified. The passage then details the conditions for valid slaughter and discusses special cases like snake slough, pig skin, and human skin. Understanding these rules is essential for determining the permissibility of using animal hides and consuming meat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule of interchangeability
Read the opening sentence: 'Every skin that is purified by tanning is purified by slaughter, and what is not, then not.' This establishes a bidirectional relationship—tanning and slaughter are interchangeable for hide purification.
This rule applies to any animal whose hide can withstand tanning (e.g., cow, sheep, goat).
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute about meat
Note the three quoted opinions from Al-Hidayah, Al-Fatawa, and Al-Nihayah regarding whether the meat is also purified by slaughter. Al-Hidayah says yes even if inedible; Al-Fatawa says no; Al-Nihayah conditions it on the animal not having impure saliva.
These are all within the Hanafi school—no single view is declared predominant here.
3
List the conditions for valid slaughter
The text specifies two conditions: (1) the slaughterer must be from 'the people of slaughter' (Muslim or Kitabi) and must mention Allah's name (tasmiyah); (2) the slaughter must be in the proper place—between the collarbone and jaw.
A Magian's slaughter is invalid; his hide requires tanning. If the place is wrong, the slaughter may not be valid.
4
Note the exceptions: snake slough, pig, and human skin
The snake's shed skin (slough) is pure, but its actual skin is impure and cannot be tanned. Pig and human skin are never purified by tanning or slaughter.
Prayer and wudu are permissible using hides of other animals after tanning, but not pig or human skin.
5
Understand that the tanner's identity does not matter
The final part states that tanning is effective regardless of whether the tanner is Muslim, kafir, child, insane, or woman.
This emphasizes that the purification by tanning is a physical process, not a ritual one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل جلد يطهر بالدباغ : فإنه يطهر بالذكاة، وما لا فلا. - وفي «الهداية ) : ما طَهُر بالدباغ: طهر بالذكاة، وكذا لحمه، في الصحيح وإن لم يكن مأكولاً. وفي «الفتاوى»: الصحيح: أنه لا يطهر لحمه. وفي «النهاية»: إنما يطهر لحمه إذا لم يكن نجس السؤر. ثم على قول صاحب الهداية»: إنما يطهر لحمه وجلده بالذكاة: إذا وجدت الذكاة الشرعية، بأن كان المذكّي من أهل الذكاة بالتسمية، أما إذا كان مجوسياً فلا بد في الجلد من الدباغ ؛ لأن فعله أمانة، لا ذكاة. ويشترط أيضاً : أن تكون الذكاة في محلّها، وهو ما بين اللبة واللحيين. - وقميص الحية(۲): طاهر، كذا قال الحلواني، وجلدها: نجس لا يطهر بالدباغة؛ لأنه لا يحتملها. وجازت الصلاة فيه والوضوء منه، إلا جلد الخنزير، والآدمي. وقوله : دبغ : فيه إشارة إلى أنه يستوي أن يكون الدابغ مسلماً، أو كافراً، أو صبياً، أو مجنوناً، أو امرأة.
And every skin that is purified by tanning, then it is purified by slaughter, and what is not, then not. And in al-Hidayah: What is purified by tanning is purified by slaughter, and likewise its meat, in the sound opinion, even if it is not edible. And in al-Fatawa: The sound opinion is that its meat is not purified. And in al-Nihayah: Its meat is only purified if it is not of impure saliva. Then according to the statement of the author of al-Hidayah: Its meat and skin are purified by slaughter only if the valid religious slaughter is found, that the slaughterer is among the people of slaughter with the tasmiyah (mentioning the name of Allah). If he is a Magian, then tanning is necessary for the skin, because his act is a trust, not a slaughter. It is also conditioned that the slaughter is in its place, which is between the collarbone and the jaw. And the snake's slough is pure, as al-Hulwani said, and its skin is impure and not purified by tanning because it cannot withstand [tanning]. And prayer is permissible in it and wudu from it, except pig skin and human skin. And his saying: 'tanned' indicates that it is equal whether the tanner is Muslim, or infidel, or child, or insane, or woman.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between purification by tanning (dabgh) and purification by slaughter (dhakah). The rule: any hide that can be purified by tanning can also be purified by slaughter, and vice versa. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the meat: al-Hidayah says the meat is also purified by slaughter even if the animal is not edible; al-Fatawa disagrees; al-Nihayah conditions that the meat is purified only if the animal's saliva is not inherently impure. The conditions for valid slaughter are then given: the slaughterer must be from those who perform valid slaughter (Muslim or Kitabi) and must mention Allah's name (tasmiyah); a Magian's slaughter is not valid, so his hide requires tanning. The place of slaughter must be between the collarbone and jaw. Special cases: the snake's shed skin (qamis) is pure, but the snake's actual skin is impure and cannot be purified by tanning because it would be damaged. Then the ruling: it is permissible to pray wearing such tanned hides and to perform wudu from containers made from them, except for pig and human skin. Finally, the tanner's identity does not affect the purification—anyone, Muslim, kafir, child, insane, or woman, can tan a hide and it becomes pure.

Key Terms
ذكاة (dhakāt): Ritual slaughter that purifies the animal for consumption and use of its hide
قميص الحية (qamīṣ al-ḥayyah): The outer skin shed by a snake, which is considered pure
مجوسي (majūsī): A Magian (Zoroastrian), whose slaughter is not valid because they are not considered Ahl al-Kitab

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A Muslim who says Bismillah
B) A Jew who says Bismillah
C) A Magian (Zoroastrian)
D) A Christian who says Bismillah

Key Takeaway
Tanning and valid slaughter are interchangeable for hide purification, but the purification of meat by slaughter is disputed among Hanafi authorities.

Dog Hide Purification by Tanning: The Hanafi Position and Shafi'i Objection
States Hanafi view on dog hide, types of tanning, and responses to objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dog hide purification by tanning, distinguish between real and legal tanning, and explain the evidence from the exception.

Core Idea
Dog skin is purified by tanning; the exception of pig and human proves it.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayira clarifies the Hanafi position that dog hide becomes pure through tanning, contrary to the Shafi'i school. The text also introduces two types of tanning and addresses a potential objection about legal tanning's durability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
What does the Hanafi school say about dog skin and tanning? State it clearly.
2
Note the opposing view
Who disagrees? The Shafi'i school and a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad within Hanafi.
3
Understand the types of tanning
Define real tanning (chemical astringents) and legal tanning (sun, earth).
4
Analyze the legal tanning wetting dispute
If a legally tanned hide is wetted, there are two narrations: one says it returns to impurity, another says not. Al-Khujandi prefers the latter.
5
Examine the evidence from the exception
The exception of pig and human skin from permissibility implies dog skin is purified by tanning.
This is the key Hanafi proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجلد الكلب يطهر بالدباغ عندنا. وقال الشافعي : لا يطهر، وهو رواية أيضاً عن الحسن بن زياد. والدباغ نوعان: حقيقي، كالسَّبِّ، والقَرَظ، وقشور الرمان، وأشباه ذلك، وحكمي: كالشمس، والتراب. فإن عاود المدبوغ بالحكمي الماء: فيه روايتان: في رواية: يعود نجساً، وفي رواية: لا يعود نجساً. قال الخُجندي: وهو الأظهر. قوله: (وجازت الصلاة عليه، والوضوء منه). هنا؟ فإن قيل: ليس هذا موضع تطهير الأعيان النجسة، فلم ذكره الشيخ قيل : لأجل قوله : والوضوء منه. قوله : (إلا جلد الخنزير، والآدمى): في هذا الاستثناء دلالة على طهارة جلد الكلب بالدباغ، وقد بيناه. وكما يطهر جلده بالدباغ، فكذا بالذكاة.
And the dog's skin is purified by tanning in our school. And al-Shafi'i said: It is not purified, and it is also a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. And tanning is of two types: real, like sumac, acacia, pomegranate peels, and the like; and legal, like the sun and earth. If the legally tanned [hide] is wetted with water, there are two narrations: in one narration, it returns to being impure; in another, it does not return to being impure. Al-Khujandi said: The latter is more apparent. His saying: 'And prayer is permissible in it and wudu from it' – here? So if it is said: This is not the place for purifying impure objects, so why did the Sheikh mention it? He said: Because of his saying: 'and wudu from it.' His saying: 'Except pig skin and human skin' – in this exception there is evidence for the purification of dog skin by tanning, and we have explained it. And just as its skin is purified by tanning, so too by slaughter.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage covers multiple connected rulings. First, the Hanafi school holds that the skin of a dog is purified by tanning, while Imam al-Shafi'i and a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (within the Hanafi school) hold that it remains impure. Then the text explains that tanning is of two types: real (using astringent materials like sumac, acacia, pomegranate peels) and legal (by exposure to sun and earth). For hides tanned by legal means, there is a dispute: if such a hide is subsequently wetted, does it become impure again? One narration says yes, another says no, with al-Khujandi preferring the view that it does not return to impurity. The passage then addresses a potential objection: why does the author mention wudu from tanned hides in a book on purification? The answer is that wudu from tanned hides is relevant because the hide itself is the issue. Finally, the exception of pig and human skin from the general permissibility is used as evidence that dog skin is purified by tanning, because if dog skin were not pure, it would have been included in the exception. The passage concludes by reiterating that dog skin is also purified by slaughter just as it is by tanning.

Key Terms
دباغ حقيقي (dabāgh ḥaqīqī): Real tanning using chemical astringents like sumac, acacia, pomegranate peels
دباغ حكمي (dabāgh ḥukmī): Legal tanning by exposure to sun and earth, considered a valid method according to some Hanafi scholars
كلب (kalb): Dog; its skin is impure when alive but can be purified by tanning in Hanafi school

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what evidence is given for the purification of dog skin by tanning?
A) A direct hadith
B) The exception of pig and human skin from permissibility of use
C) Consensus of all scholars
D) The condition of slaughter

Key Takeaway
Dog skin is purified by tanning in the Hanafi school, supported by the exception of pig and human skin.

Purification by Tanning: Hanafi Rules and the Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling on tanning with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tanning, identify the exceptions, explain the dispute with Shafi'i on nominal tanning, and apply the ruling to cases like dog skin and elephant.

Core Idea
Real tanning purifies all skins except pig and human; nominal tanning is insufficient.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the Hanafi position on tanning animal skins. The ruling is that tanning purifies the skin of any dead animal (except pig and human) regardless of the tanner's identity. The passage also addresses a key dispute with the Shafi'i school regarding 'nominal' tanning (e.g., sun-drying) and an internal Hanafi disagreement on elephant skin. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying purity rules in daily worship.

Guided Path
1
State the Main Ruling
Identify the act (tanning) and its effect on the skin. The text says prayer and ablution are permissible with tanned skins, except pig and human.
The ruling applies to all skins of permissible animals, including dogs in the Hanafi school.
2
Note the Condition of the Tanner
The tanner's religion, age, gender, or sanity does not affect the validity of tanning. The process itself is what matters.
This is a relaxation compared to some views that require a Muslim tanner.
3
Distinguish Real vs. Nominal Tanning
Real tanning uses chemical agents (e.g., bark, sumac) and purifies. Nominal tanning (e.g., sun, earth) does not purify according to the Hanafi school.
This is the point of disagreement with al-Shafi'i.
4
Analyze the Dispute with Shafi'i
Shafi'i considers both types valid. The Hanafi response relies on a narration that nominal tanning is insufficient, and they present two narrations on whether nominally tanned skin returns to impurity when wet.
Al-Khujandi preferred the view that it does not return to impurity.
5
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Elephant
Muhammad al-Shaybani treats elephant skin like pig (not purified), while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule it purified by real tanning and bones permissible.
The mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

And his statement: 'tanned' indicates that it is the same whether the tanner is Muslim, disbeliever, child, insane, or woman. And the skin of the dog is purified by tanning according to us. Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the purification of animal skins by tanning. The author first states the main ruling: prayer in tanned skin and ablution from it are permissible, except for pig and human skin. The commentary expands on the condition of the tanner: it may be any person, Muslim or not, adult or child, sane or insane, male or female, indicating that the validity of tanning does not depend on the religious status or maturity of the tanner. Then it emphasizes that dog skin is purified by tanning in the Hanafi school. The Shafi'i objection is presented: they consider that nominal tanning by sun or earth also purifies. The Hanafi refutation is implied by stating that nominal tanning does not purify (based on a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad). The passage also addresses a secondary issue: if a nominally tanned skin gets wet, there are two narrations—one says it returns to impurity, another says it does not; al-Khujandi prefers the latter. The commentator explains why the author mentioned tanning in this context: because the ruling on ablution from it necessitated it. The exception of pig and human skin is then explained as evidence that dog skin is purified by tanning. The order of mentioning pig before human is justified by the principle of starting with the more humiliated object when mentioning destruction, supported by a Quranic verse. Finally, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding elephant skin and bones: Imam Muhammad holds it is like the pig (skin not purified, bones impure), while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the skin is purified and bones are permissible.

Key Terms
دبغ (dibagh): The process of treating animal skin with tanning agents to render it pure.
دابغ (dabigh): The person performing tanning.
حكمي (hukmi): Nominal tanning, e.g., by exposure to sun or earth.
حقيقي (haqiqi): Real tanning using effective chemical agents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Animal skins are purified by real tanning except pig and human; nominal tanning is not accepted in the Hanafi school.

Purity of Hair and Bones of a Dead Animal (Maytah)
Ruling that hair, bones, hoofs, sinews, and horns of a dead animal are pure except pig.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the purity of hair, bones, and similar parts of a dead animal, identify the conditions and exceptions, and apply it to real-life scenarios.

Core Idea
Hair and bones of a dead animal are pure (except pig) when dry; they may be used due to necessity.

A common assumption is that every part of a dead animal (maytah) is impure. However, the Hanafi school makes a key distinction: parts that are devoid of life and flowing blood—such as hair, bones, hoofs, sinews, and horns—are considered pure (tahir) when dry, except those of the pig. This ruling has practical implications for everyday items like brushes, buttons, and tools. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its conditions, and its exception for leatherworkers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts covered
List the specific animal parts mentioned in the text: hair, bones, hoofs, sinews, and horns of a dead animal (maytah).
The text explicitly mentions 'hair' and 'bone' but the commentary includes other similar parts by extension.
2
State the general ruling
According to the Hanafi school, these parts are pure (tahir) and can be used for permissible purposes, provided two conditions are met: the animal is not a pig, and the part is dry (no moisture).
The ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah. The impurity of the pig is absolute; other animals are covered.
3
Note the exception for leatherworkers
The text permits the use of the hair of a dead animal for leatherworkers (kharrāzīn) due to necessity (darurah), as no adequate substitute exists. This is a special dispensation.
This dispensation does not extend to other parts or for non-essential uses.
4
Compare with other schools
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) generally consider all parts of a dead animal impure. The Hanafi position relies on the distinction between parts that contain flowing blood (impure) and those that do not.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; both positions are based on valid proofs.

Source Text

أراد ما سوى الخنزير، ولم تكن عليه رطوبة.

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling that the hair, bones, hoofs, sinews, and horns of a dead animal (maytah) are pure (tahir). The commentary specifies that this applies to animals other than the pig, and on the condition that these parts are dry (no moisture). It also notes that the hair is permitted for use by leatherworkers (kharrāzīn) due to necessity, as no adequate substitute exists. This is an exception to the general rule that parts of a dead animal are impure; the Hanafi school distinguishes parts that are without soul and do not contain flowing blood.

Key Terms
الميتة (al-maytah): A dead animal that was not slaughtered according to Islamic law.
شعر (sha'r): Hair of a dead animal, which is considered pure by Hanafis.
خرازين (kharrāzīn): Leatherworkers or craftsmen who use animal hair for sewing or ornamentation.

Key Takeaway

Purity of Hair and Bones from Maytah: Hanafi Rulings and Inter-Madhhab Comparison
شرح حكم طهارة شعر وعظم الميتة مع خلاف المذاهب

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hair and bones of maytah, identify the condition of being shaved/sheared, and compare the Shafi'i and Maliki positions.

Core Idea
Hair and bones of dead animals (except pig) are pure, but only if dry and shaved — not plucked.

In this passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayra, the commentator expands on the matn's ruling that hair, bones, and similar parts of a dead animal are pure. He clarifies the condition that they must be free of moisture and not plucked. He also discusses intra-Hanafi differences on the elephant and on human hair, and compares the views of Imam Muhammad, Abu Hanifa, and the Sahibayn. Finally, he presents the Shafi'i and Maliki positions, making this a rich inter-madhhab comparison.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general Hanafi ruling
Locate the statement 'hair, bones, hoof, tendon, and horn are pure'. Note that the exception is the pig.
The default ruling is purity due to the principle of original cleanliness.
2
Recognize conditions for purity
Note that purity is contingent on the part being dry and shaved or sheared. If plucked or wet, it becomes impure.
This condition applies to hair, wool, etc., but not to bones.
3
Examine intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Find the dispute on elephant: Muhammad says elephant is like pig (impure), Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say its bones are pure. Also note the two narrations from Muhammad on human hair and nails.
The mufta bihi is that human hair/nails are pure when shaved.
4
Compare with other madhhabs
Read the positions: Shafi'i considers all parts of maytah impure; Maliki considers bones impure but hair pure.
The Hanafi position is the most permissive in this regard.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and basis
Identify that the relied-upon position is the purity of hair and bones (except pig) based on default purity, necessity for leather workers, and the tarjih of al-Karkhi on human hair.
The authority weight is zahir riwayah.

The hair of a dead animal, and its bone, and its hoof, and its tendon, and its horn: are pure. And he mentioned the pig before the human being because it is a place of humiliation, and in a place of humiliation the humiliated is given precedence. Like the saying of the Most High: "...monasteries and churches and synagogues in which the name of Allah is mentioned much..." (Al-Hajj 40). So He mentioned monasteries and churches before mosques because of the mention of demolition, because it is humiliation. And 'biya' is the plural of 'bi'a' with a kasra on the ba, which is for the Christians. And 'sawami' are for the Sabians. And 'salawat' are the churches of the Jews, and they used to call them in Hebrew 'salawat'. And the elephant is like the pig according to Muhammad: its skin is not purified by tanning, and its bones are impure, its sale and use are not permissible. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: there is no harm in its bones, and its skin is purified by tanning. Thus in 'Al-Khujandi'. * His saying: (And the hair of a dead animal and its bone are pure). He intended other than the pig, and there is no moisture on it. According to them. And he permitted its hair for the leather workers out of necessity because nothing else takes its place. (1) And now that there is no longer a necessity, its use is not permissible. See Ibn 'Abidin 5/73. And from Abu Yusuf: that he disliked it also for them. And its sale is not permissible in all narrations. And feathers, wool, fur, horn, hoof, cloven hoof, and hoof – all of these are pure from the dead animal, except the pig. And this is if the hair is shaved or sheared; but if it is plucked, then it is impure. Likewise, the hair of a human being, if it is shaved or sheared, it is pure; if it is plucked, it is impure. And from Muhammad regarding the impurity of human hair, nail, and bone, there are two narrations. Al-Maturidi took its purity, and Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar took its purity, and al-Karkhi relied on it, and it is the correct opinion. According to al-Shafi'i: the hair and bone of a dead animal are impure. According to Malik: its bone is impure, and its hair is pure. And the Shaykh did not mention the egg and milk of a dead animal, so we say: If a chicken dies and an egg comes out of it after its death, it is pure.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the matn statement regarding the purity of parts from a dead animal (maytah). The commentary begins by affirming the general ruling: hair, bones, hooves, tendons, and horns of a dead animal are pure (tahir). The author then explains why the pig (khinzir) is mentioned before humans in the preceding discussion: because it is an object of humiliation, and in contexts of humiliation, the humiliated entity is given precedence. He supports this with a Quranic verse where monasteries and churches are mentioned before mosques because the context is destruction. The passage then addresses the elephant: according to Imam Muhammad, the elephant is like the pig — its skin is not purified by tanning and its bones are impure. However, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the elephant's bones are permissible and its skin is purified by tanning. The commentator notes that the matn's ruling on hair and bone of maytah applies only to animals other than the pig and provided there is no moisture on them. There was a license (rukhsah) for leather workers to use the hair of dead animals out of necessity, but now that necessity no longer exists, it is not permissible. Abu Yusuf reportedly disliked it even for them. The sale of such hair is prohibited in all accepted narrations. Feathers, wool, fur, horns, hooves, and the like are all pure from any dead animal except the pig. However, there is a condition: if the hair is shaved or sheared, it is pure; if plucked, it is impure because it retains moisture from the carcass. Similarly, human hair is pure if shaved or sheared, but impure if plucked. There is a difference of narration from Imam Muhammad regarding the impurity of human hair, nails, and bones: one narration says they are impure (taken by al-Maturidi), another says they are pure (taken by al-Saffar and relied upon by al-Karkhi, which is considered correct). The passage then presents the positions of other schools: al-Shafi'i holds that both hair and bones of a dead animal are impure; Malik holds that bones are impure but hair is pure. Finally, the commentator addresses something not mentioned by the author of the matn: the egg and milk of a dead animal. Using the example of a chicken that dies and lays an egg after death, the ruling is that the egg is pure. This is based on the principle that what is separated from a living animal is pure unless there is specific evidence of impurity.

Key Terms
ميتة (maytah): An animal that died without being ritually slaughtered (dhabh).
طاهر (tahir): Ritually pure, not requiring purification.
دباغ (dibagh): Tanning process that purifies the skin of a dead animal (except pig).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is impure because it comes from a dead animal.
B) It is pure only if the hair is shaved or sheared, not plucked.
C) It is pure regardless of how it is removed.
D) It is impure unless it is from a cow that was slaughtered.

Key Takeaway
Hair and bones of maytah are pure in the Hanafi school, provided they are dry and shaved/sheared; this ruling differs from other madhhabs and has practical implications for use and sale.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Milk from a Dead Sheep
Intra-Hanafi dispute on milk from a dead sheep

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on milk from a dead sheep, identify the principle of impurity transfer from the vessel, and determine the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: impurity of vessel does not transfer; Sahibayn: it does.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on the purity of milk found in the udder of a dead sheep. The disagreement hinges on whether the impurity of the container (the udder) affects the milk contained within. Understanding this dispute is key to grasping how Hanafi legal reasoning accounts for the transmission of impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
State the rulings of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the milk: Abu Hanifa holds it is pure and permissible to drink; the Sahibayn hold it is pure in itself but becomes impure by the vessel, so drinking is impermissible.
All three agree the milk is pure in itself.
2
Locate the point of agreement
All three agree the milk is pure in itself because death does not dissolve it (لا يحله الموت). This is the common proof.
This proof is based on the nature of milk.
3
Determine the point of disagreement
Abu Hanifa says the vessel's impurity does not transfer to the milk; the Sahibayn say it does. The practical consequence: Abu Hanifa allows drinking; the Sahibayn forbid it.
The vessel is the udder, which is considered impure due to death.
4
Analyze the proof
The common proof 'لا يحله الموت' is used by Abu Hanifa to argue that milk remains pure regardless of the vessel. The Sahibayn accept this proof for the milk itself but argue the vessel transfers impurity.
Both sides use rational reasoning (talil).
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi fatwa works rely on Abu Hanifa's view: the milk remains pure and permissible to drink.
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah and others prefer Abu Hanifa's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ماتت شاة فخرج من ضرعها لبن : قال أبو حنيفة: هو طاهر، يحل شربه، ولا ينجس بنجاسة الوعاء (٢). وعندهما: هو طاهر في نفسه؛ لأنه لا يَحلُّه الموتُ، إلا أنه ينجس بنجاسة الوعاء، فلا يحل شربه. وعند الشافعي(۳): هو نجس، فلا يَحِلُّ شربه.
And if a sheep dies and milk comes out from its udder: Abu Hanifa said: it is pure, drinking it is permissible, and it does not become najis by the impurity of the vessel. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): it is pure in itself because death does not dissolve it, but it becomes najis by the impurity of the vessel, so drinking it is not permissible. According to al-Shafi'i: it is najis, so drinking it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute and also an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the milk of a dead sheep. The ruling concerns the purity and permissibility of drinking milk found in the udder after the animal's death. Abu Hanifa holds that the milk is pure (tahir) and permissible to drink, and it does not become impure (najis) even if the udder (the vessel) is impure because the milk is not rendered impure by the impurity of the container. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree that the milk is pure in itself because death does not affect it (i.e., it is not subject to the rules of carcass impurity), but they argue that it becomes impure by contact with the impure udder, making drinking impermissible. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the milk itself is impure, thus drinking it is impermissible. The Hanafi position is based on the principle that milk does not become impure by death (since it is a liquid that flows), but the Sahibayn consider the udder as a source of impurity due to being part of the carcass. The practical consequence: according to Abu Hanifa, one may drink the milk; according to the Sahibayn, one may not, but the milk is still pure in itself for other uses.

Key Terms
الوعاء (al-wi'aa): The container, here referring to the udder (ضرع) of the sheep.
طاهر (tahir): Pure, ritually clean, not najis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, why is drinking milk from a dead sheep not permissible?
A) Because the milk itself is impure.
B) Because the milk becomes impure by contact with the udder.
C) Because death dissolves the milk.
D) Because the sheep died a natural death.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's view that impurity of a vessel does not transfer to its contents is the relied-upon Hanafi position for milk from a dead animal.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i: Purity of Milk from a Dead Animal
Cross-madhhab dispute on milk from a dead sheep

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof that milk from a dead sheep remains pure and contrast it with the Shafi'i proof that it is impure.

Core Idea
Hanafi: milk is not dissolved by death; Shafi'i: milk is part of the carcass and thus impure.

This lesson examines the cross-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is on the same issue. The Hanafis rely on the rational proof that death does not 'dissolve' milk, meaning it retains its original purity. The Shafi'is consider the milk as part of the carcass, making it impure by extension. Understanding this difference illuminates the broader usul principle of how rulings on carcasses apply to separable liquids.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Hanafi (Abu Hanifa): milk is pure and permissible to drink. Shafi'i: milk is impure (najis) and impermissible to drink.
The Sahibayn also forbid drinking but for a different reason.
2
Identify the Hanafi proof
The proof is 'لا يحله الموت' (death does not dissolve it). This means the milk remains in its natural state of purity, unaffected by the animal's death.
This is a rational proof (talil) based on the nature of milk.
3
Identify the Shafi'i proof
The Shafi'i position is that the milk is part of the carcass, and since the carcass is impure (najis), the milk shares that impurity.
This is based on the general principle that everything from a dead animal is impure unless specified otherwise.
4
Analyze the underlying principle
Hanafi distinguishes between liquids that are not integral to the carcass and are not affected by death. Shafi'i applies the general rule that all parts of a carcass are impure.
The difference is in how they treat separable liquids.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Hanafi allows drinking the milk; Shafi'i forbids it entirely (the milk is najis and cannot be used for anything).
The thamarah al-khilaf is significant: permissibility vs. prohibition of consumption.

According to al-Shafi'i: it is najis, so drinking it is not permissible.

Rennet from a Dead Kid: Hanafi and Shafi'i Views
Explains Hanafi and Shafi'i rulings on rennet from a dead kid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on rennet from a dead kid, contrast it with the Shafi'i ruling, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference and the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Rennet from a dead kid is pure and permissible in the Hanafi school, unlike in the Shafi'i school.

This lesson examines a practical issue of taharah: the ruling on rennet (infaḥah) from a kid that died without slaughter. The rennet is used in cheese-making, so the ruling affects what Muslims may consume. The Hanafi school has a nuanced position, with Abu Hanifa differing from his two disciples, while the Shafi'i school takes a stricter view. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies the legal reasoning behind purity rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa: the rennet and its contents are pure and permissible to eat, whether liquid or solid.
Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State the Shafi'i ruling: rennet from a dead animal is impure and impermissible to eat.
Imam al-Shafi'i considers any part of a dead animal as najis.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof: the general principle that a dead animal's parts are impure (based on Quran 5:3 and hadith).
The Shafi'i school applies this to the stomach and its contents.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes rennet as a secretion like milk; the stomach is a container, not inherently impure. Death does not affect it.
The Sahibayn further distinguish solid vs liquid, but Abu Hanifa's view is broader.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis permit cheese made from rennet of a dead kid (after washing if solid per Sahibayn), while Shafi'is prohibit it entirely.
This difference affects daily food choices for Muslims of different madhahib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن مات جدي: فإنفَحَتُه (٤) طاهرة، يجوز أكل ما في جوفها، سواء كان مائعاً أو جامداً عند أبي حنيفة. وعندهما: إن كان مائعاً : لا يجوز، وإن كان جامداً وغسل: جاز أكله. وعند الشافعي): لا يجوز أكله. والإنفحة بكسر الهمزة، وفتح الفاء مخففة: كَرِسُ الجَدي ما لم يأكل.
And if a kid dies: its rennet is pure; it is permissible to eat what is in its stomach, whether liquid or solid, according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): if it is liquid, it is not permissible; if it is solid and washed, it is permissible to eat. According to al-Shafi'i: it is not permissible to eat it. And 'al-infaḥah' (with kasra on the hamza, fatḥa on the fa, lightened) is the stomach of the kid that has not eaten.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the rennet (infaḥa) of a dead kid. Rennet is the curdled milk found in the stomach of a suckling kid that has not yet eaten solid food. It is used to coagulate milk in cheese-making. Abu Hanifa rules that the rennet and its contents (the liquid or solid in the stomach) are pure and permissible to eat, regardless of whether it is liquid or solid. The Sahibayn distinguish: if the contents are liquid, they are not permissible (because liquids are more susceptible to impurity), but if solid, after washing, it becomes permissible. Imam al-Shafi'i forbids eating it entirely, considering it najis. The text also defines 'infaḥa' as the stomach (karish) of a kid that has not eaten. The Hanafi position relies on the principle that death does not render parts that are not inherently impure (like milk or rennet) as najis, though the Sahibayn hold that liquids are affected by the carcass's impurity.

Key Terms
الإنفحة (al-infaḥah): The stomach (كرش) of a kid that has not yet eaten solid food; it contains rennet used to coagulate milk.
كرش (karish): The stomach or rumen of an animal, specifically of a kid that has not eaten.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, regardless of state.
B) Only if it is liquid.
C) Only if it is solid and washed.
D) Only if the kid was slaughtered.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, rennet from a dead kid is pure and permissible to eat, following Abu Hanifa's view, which is the relied-upon position.

Purification of a Well When Najasah Falls In
States the ruling on purifying a well when impurity falls in.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling (wajib) for purifying a well when a liquid impurity or the death of a small animal occurs, and identify the required action (nazh) and its conditions.

Core Idea
A well is purified by completely draining its water (nazh) when impurity falls in.

In Hanafi fiqh, wells are a common water source, and their purification after contamination is essential for maintaining taharah. This lesson covers the basic ruling from the matn: when a liquid impurity or the death of certain small animals occurs, the well must be completely emptied. Let's examine the conditions and the act required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act required for purification is nazh – complete draining of all water from the well.
2
Identify the ruling
The ruling is wajib (obligatory); the well must be emptied.
This is not fard because the evidence is zanni.
3
Identify the triggers
Two triggers: (1) falling of any liquid impurity (e.g., urine, blood), (2) death of specific small animals (mouse, sparrow, etc.) inside the well.
The list is exemplary; the principle applies to similar animals.
4
Identify the conditions
The impurity must have contacted the water, and the dead animal must be removed before nazh.
Removing the animal is a condition, not part of nazh itself.
5
Note the completeness requirement
Nazh must be complete; partial removal leaves the well impure.
This is the key point for memorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا وقعت في البئر نجاسةٌ : نُزحت ، وكان نزح ما فيها من الماء طهارة لها . فإن ماتت فيها فأرةٌ، أو عُصفورةٌ، أو صَعُوةٌ، أو سودانيةٌ، أو سَام أبرص :
And if an impurity falls into the well: it is drained, and draining what water is in it is purification for it. If a mouse, or a sparrow, or a صعوة (a small bird), or a سودانية (a type of bird), or a سام أبرص (a gecko) dies in it:
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage from the matn (text) states the general rule for purifying a well when a liquid impurity (like urine or blood) falls into it: the well must be completely emptied (nazh) of water. This emptying itself serves as purification (طهارة) for the well, meaning the water is removed and the well becomes pure. The second part lists specific small animals whose death in the well triggers the same ruling: a mouse, a sparrow, a صعوة (a small bird), سودانية (probably a blackbird), and سام أبرص (a gecko). The text does not specify the number of buckets to be removed; the general principle is that all water must be drained. The commentary (not included fully here) elaborates that the drainage must occur after removing the dead animal, and that the process purifies not only the water but also the well's walls, bucket, rope, and the hands of the one drawing water.

Key Terms
نزح (nazh): The act of completely draining or emptying the well of its water.
سام أبرص (saam abras): A type of large gecko or lizard (وزغ كبير) that is considered a pest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Remove the mouse and then pray over the well.
B) Remove the mouse and drain all the water from the well.
C) Remove only the water around the mouse.
D) Add pure water to dilute the impurity.

Key Takeaway
Complete draining (nazh) is wajib for purifying a well contaminated by liquid impurity or the death of small animals.

Purification of a Well When a Mouse Dies: Rulings and Dispute
Ruling on purifying a well when an animal dies, with amounts of water to draw and a Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for purifying a well when a mouse dies, explain how the amount of water drawn changes based on the mouse's condition, and compare the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on multiple mice.

Core Idea
For a dead mouse in a well, draw 20-30 buckets normally, but all water if it fled from a cat.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on purifying a well when a mouse (or similar small animal) dies inside. The passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar details the obligatory and recommended amounts of water to be drawn, introduces an important condition that escalates the requirement, and records a classic intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on how to handle multiple mice. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly applying the rules of taharah in everyday situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and base ruling
The act is drawing water (nazḥ) from the well after the dead animal is removed. For a single mouse (and similar animals), between 20 and 30 buckets must be drawn – 20 are obligatory (wajib) and 10 are recommended (mustahabb).
2
Note the condition that changes the ruling
If the mouse was fleeing from a cat or wounded, then all water must be drawn, even if it came out alive. This is because a frightened mouse would have urinated, adding liquid impurity to the well.
This principle also applies to a cat fleeing from a dog.
3
Understand consensus and small animals
For animals smaller than a mouse (sparrow, finch, etc.), the same ruling as a mouse applies. There is consensus that two mice are treated as one, and two cats require all water.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on multiple mice
Abu Yusuf holds that 5-9 mice are like a cat (all water), 10+ like a dog (all water). Muhammad holds that 3 mice are like a cat, 6 like a dog, up to 9. Both use qiyas but differ on thresholds.
The text does not specify a tarjih, but later fatwa prefers caution.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
Animals between a mouse and a cat (e.g., pigeon) follow the mouse ruling; between a cat and a dog follow the cat ruling (all water). This analogical extension shows Hanafi method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
وإذا وقعت في البئر نجاسةٌ : نُزحت ، وكان نزح ما فيها من الماء طهارة لها .
فإن ماتت فيها فأرةٌ، أو عُصفورةٌ، أو صَعُوةٌ، أو سودانيةٌ، أو سَام أبرص :
والخمر.
تطهير الآبار حال وقوع النجاسة فيها : ]

قوله: (وكان نزح ما فيها من الماء: طهارة لها):
فيه إشارة إلى أنه يطهر الوَحَلُ، والأحجار، والدلو، والرشاء، ويد النازح.
كيفية تطهير الآبار إن مات فيها حيوان :]
قوله: (فإن ماتت فيها فأرةٌ أو عصفور أو صعوة أو سودانية).
إنما يكون النزح بعد إخراج الفأرة، أما ما دامت فيها: فلا يُعتد بشيء من النزح. قوله: (أو سام أبرص) : بتشديد الميم : الوَزَغُ الكبير، وهما اسمان جُعِلا اسماً واحداً، فإن شئت أعربت الأول وأضفت إلى الثاني، وإن شئت بنيت الأول على الفتح، وأعربت الثاني بإعراب ما لا ينصرف، وإن شئت بنيتهما جميعاً على الفتح، مثل: خمسة عشر.
نزح منها ما بين عشرين دلواً إلى ثلاثين دلواً،
قوله : (نزح منها ما بين عشرين دلواً إلى ثلاثين):
العشرون بطريق الإيجاب، والعشرة بطريق الاستحباب
وهذا إذا لم تكن الفأرة هاربةً من الهرة، ولا مجروحةً، أما إذا كانت كذلك : يُنزح جميع الماء وإن خرجت حيةً ؛ لأنها تبول إذا كانت هاربة. - وكذا الهرة إذا كانت هاربة من الكلب، أو مجروحة: يُنزح كلُّ الماء؛ لأن البول والدم نجاسة مائعة.

والعشر: كالكلب، وهذا عند أبي يوسف.
