The Book of Purification
And if impurity falls into the well, it is drained, and draining the water in it is purification for it.
If a mouse, or a sparrow, or a finch, or a blackbird, or a gecko dies in it:
And wine.
Purification of wells when impurity falls into them:
His saying: (And if impurity falls into the well): meaning liquid like urine and blood.
His saying: (is drained): means the well, and what is intended is its water. He mentioned the place but intended the occupant, as it is said: the river flowed, and the spout ran, and from it is the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And ask the town' (Yusuf 12:82).
His saying: (And that draining what is in it of water is purification for it):
There is an indication in it that the mud, the stones, the bucket, the rope, and the hand of the drainer become pure.
The manner of purifying wells if an animal dies in it:
His saying: (If a mouse or a sparrow or a finch or a blackbird dies in it):
The draining is only after removing the mouse; as long as it is in the well, no amount of draining is counted. His saying: (or a gecko): with a shaddah on the meem: the large house lizard, and they are two names made into one name; if you wish you may decline the first and add it to the second, and if you wish you may build the first on the fatha and decline the second as something that is not declined, and if you wish you may build both of them on the fatha, like: fifteen.
There are drawn from it between twenty buckets and thirty buckets.
His saying: (There are drawn from it between twenty buckets and thirty):
Twenty is obligatory, and ten is recommended.
And this is if the mouse is not fleeing from a cat, nor wounded; but if it is so, all the water is drained even if it came out alive, because it urinates when fleeing. And likewise the cat if it is fleeing from a dog, or wounded: all the water is drained, because urine and blood are liquid impurity.
And the ruling of two mice, three, and four is like that of one.
And five is like a cat up to nine.
And ten is like a dog, and this is according to Abu Yusuf.
And Muhammad said: Three is like a cat, and six is like a dog, up to nine.
And likewise the sparrow and what is like it.
And as for two mice: like one mouse, by consensus.
And regarding two cats: all the water is drained, by consensus.
And what is between a mouse and a cat: its ruling is the ruling of a mouse.
And what is between a cat and a dog: like a cat.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, dealing with the purification of a well when impurity falls into it or an animal dies inside. The commentator first explains that if a liquid impurity like urine or blood falls into the well, the entire water must be drained, and this draining purifies the well itself, its mud, stones, bucket, rope, and the hand of the drainer. The ruling specifies that the draining is only effective after the dead animal is removed; drawing water while it remains is not counted.

The passage then details the amount of water to be drawn for different animals: for a mouse, sparrow, finch, blackbird, or gecko, between twenty and thirty buckets are drawn—twenty being obligatory and ten recommended. However, if the mouse is fleeing from a cat or wounded, then all the water must be drawn, even if it emerges alive, because such a mouse would have urinated out of fear. Similarly, a cat fleeing from a dog or wounded requires all water to be drawn.

An important intra-Hanafi dispute is recorded: according to Abu Yusuf, five mice up to nine mice are treated like a cat (all water drawn), and ten mice are like a dog (all water drawn). Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that three mice are like a cat and six mice are like a dog, up to nine. Beyond that, both agree on the rulings for two mice (same as one), two cats (all water drawn), and animals smaller than a mouse (like sparrow) follow the mouse's ruling. The commentator also notes consensus on certain points: two mice are like one, and two cats require draining all water.

This ruling demonstrates the Hanafi method of analogical reasoning (qiyas) based on the size and state of the animal, and the careful consideration of circumstances like fear and injury.

Key Terms
نزح (nazḥ): Draining or drawing water from the well to purify it after impurity falls in.
فأرة (fa'rah): Mouse; a small animal whose death in a well requires drawing 20-30 buckets of water under normal conditions.
سام أبرص (sām abraṣ): A large gecko or house lizard; treated similarly to a mouse in ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mouse dies in a well and it was fleeing from a cat, how much water must be drawn?
A) 20 buckets
B) 30 buckets
C) All the water
D) Nothing, because it came out alive

Key Takeaway
The ruling for purifying a well depends on the animal's size and condition; always check if the animal was fleeing or wounded, as that determines whether 20-30 buckets or all water is required.

Rulings on Animals in Wells and Intra-Hanafi Disputes
Explains rulings for mouse, cat, dog in well and disagreements between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for a mouse falling into a well and distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding numerical thresholds for cats/dogs and purification after well drying.

Core Idea
A mouse in a well requires 20 buckets; but numbers and circumstances change the ruling, and Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on when a cat or dog triggers full removal.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira elaborates on the well-known ruling for a mouse falling into a well. However, it quickly moves into detailed situations and internal disagreements within the Hanafi school. Understanding these nuances is crucial for applying the correct ruling when multiple animals fall, when animals are fleeing, or when the well dries up. This lesson will guide you through the base ruling, the extensions, and the points of ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and its conditions
State the default nazh amount for a non-fleeing, non-injured mouse (20 buckets wajib, 10 mustahab). Note that two, three, four mice are still like one.
This is the foundation from al-Quduri's matn.
2
Recognize the exception for fleeing/injured animals
If the mouse is fleeing from a cat or injured, all water must be drawn because it may have urinated. The same applies to a cat fleeing from a dog or injured.
The key principle: liquid impurity (urine/blood) contaminates the entire well.
3
Understand the numerical thresholds for multiple animals
Abu Yusuf: 5 mice = cat (all water), 10 mice = dog (all water). Muhammad: 3 mice = cat, 6 mice = dog. Up to nine, then the pattern repeats.
The difference stems from the effective cause (illa) for equivalence.
4
Map the ikhtilaf on purification after the last bucket
Abu Yusuf requires the bucket to leave the well's top; Muhammad considers separation from the water sufficient. Also, if the well dries, Abu Yusuf says nazh is still needed; Muhammad says drying purifies.
The correct view for partial drying is that nazh is necessary (al-sahih).
5
Apply the rulings to a scenario
Given a specific case (e.g., three mice, one injured), determine the required nazh according to each imam and the mufta bihi.
Practice consolidating the rules and ikhtilaf.

Draw from it between twenty buckets and thirty buckets. If you wish, you treat the first as having fatḥa, and you inflect the second with the declension of a non-diptote; and if you wish, you treat both as having fatḥa, like 'fifteen'. His statement: (Draw from it between twenty buckets and thirty): the twenty are by way of obligation, and the ten by way of recommendation. This is if the mouse is not fleeing from the cat, nor injured; but if it is like that, all the water is drawn even if it comes out alive, because it urinates when fleeing. And likewise the cat if it is fleeing from the dog, or injured: all water is drawn, because urine and blood are liquid impurity. The ruling for two, three, and four mice is like one. Five are like a cat up to nine; ten are like a dog — this according to Abū Yūsuf. Muḥammad said: three are like a cat, six are like a dog, up to nine. Likewise the sparrow and what is in its meaning. As for two mice: they are like one mouse by consensus. For two cats: all water is drawn by consensus. What is between a mouse and a cat: its ruling is the ruling of a mouse. What is between a cat and a dog: like a cat. And thus always its ruling is the ruling of the smaller. If a cat took a mouse and they both fell into the well: if the cat is alive and the mouse dead, twenty buckets are drawn. If both are dead, drawing forty suffices them, and the lesser is included in the greater. If both are alive, they are removed and nothing is drawn. If the mouse is injured or has urinated, all water is drawn. Does the well become purified by the last bucket when it separates from the water, or until it moves away from the top of the well? According to Abū Yūsuf, until it moves away from the top of the well; according to Muḥammad, by separation from the water. Its benefit: if one takes from the well water after its separation from the water before it moves away from the top of the well: according to Abū Yūsuf it is impure, according to Muḥammad it is pure. If the well water recedes and dries after the mouse falls, or other, before drawing, and returns: it is not purified except by drawing according to Abū Yūsuf. According to Muḥammad, it is purified by drying, so that if a man prays in its bottom, his prayer is valid according to Muḥammad, contrary to Abū Yūsuf. Then if the water recedes and its bottom does not dry until the water returns, the scholars differed concerning it according to Muḥammad's statement, and the correct view is that drawing is necessary.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the rulings for animals falling into a well, focusing on the required amount of water to be drawn (nazh). It is an excerpt from the commentary on al-Quduri's matn. The passage begins with a grammatical note about the construction of the number phrase in the matn. Then it addresses the substantive fiqh: for a mouse falling into a well, drawing twenty buckets is wajib (obligatory) and ten additional buckets are mustahab (recommended), provided the mouse was not fleeing from a cat nor injured. If it was fleeing or injured, all water must be drawn because it may have urinated. The same applies to a cat fleeing from a dog or injured. The passage then presents the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the number of animals that trigger different rulings. According to Abu Yusuf, five mice are like one cat (all water drawn up to nine mice), and ten mice are like a dog (all water drawn). Muhammad says three mice are like a cat, and six like a dog. There is consensus that two mice are like one, and two cats require all water. The rule for animals between mouse and cat size follows the smaller. The passage also covers scenarios of a cat carrying a mouse, and the point at which the well becomes pure after drawing the last bucket: Abu Yusuf requires the bucket to leave the well's top, Muhammad considers separation from the water sufficient. If the well dries up after contamination, Abu Yusuf says drawing is still required; Muhammad says drying purifies it. But if the bottom does not dry completely, the correct view is that drawing is necessary.

Key Terms
نزح (nazḥ): Drawing water from a well, specifically the required amount to purify it after contamination.
فأرة (fa'ra): Mouse or rat; considered a light impurity when falling into a well, requiring limited nazh.
هرة (hirra): Cat; considered a heavier impurity than mouse, generally requiring all water to be drawn if falling into a well.
دلوا (dalw): Bucket; a unit of measurement for water drawn, typically a standard-sized bucket.
إيجاب (ījāb): Making something wajib; here indicating that twenty buckets are obligatory.
استحباب (istiḥbāb): Recommendation; here ten additional buckets are mustahab (recommended) but not obligatory.

C) All the water
D) Ten buckets

Key Takeaway
A mouse in a well requires 20 wajib and 10 mustahab buckets, but this changes with fleeing, injury, or multiple animals, and Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on the numerical equivalencies for cats and dogs.

Purification of a Well After a Cat and Mouse Fall In: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Detailed rulings and intra-Hanafi dispute on well purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on well purification when a cat and mouse fall in, distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the moment of purification, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of this dispute.

Core Idea
Purifying a well after an animal falls in depends on the animal's state; the moment of purification is disputed between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayira presents detailed scenarios for well purification when a cat and mouse fall in together. It not only gives the number of buckets to draw but also records a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding when the well becomes pure after drawing. Understanding this dispute illustrates how differences in legal reasoning lead to practical consequences in daily worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal states and bucket counts
Read the initial rulings: what is the required number of buckets when both are dead, when the cat is alive, when both are alive, and when the mouse is wounded or urinates?
The number varies from zero to all water.
2
Understand the dispute on when purification occurs
Abu Yusuf holds that the well becomes pure only when the last bucket is removed from the wellhead. Muhammad holds that it becomes pure upon separation of the last bucket from the water. Note the different thresholds.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school.
3
Apply the dispute to a concrete scenario
Consider water taken after the last bucket separates but before it is removed from the wellhead. According to Abu Yusuf this water is impure; according to Muhammad it is pure. This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
The practical difference affects the validity of wudu or use of that water.
4
Analyze the drying case and the later preferred view
If the well dries after the animal falls but before drawing, Abu Yusuf requires drawing when water returns; Muhammad considers the well purified by drying. The passage ends by stating that the correct view is that drawing is necessary when the bottom does not dry.
Later scholars favored drawing as a precaution.

If a cat took a mouse and both fell into the well: if the cat is alive and the mouse dead, twenty buckets are drawn. If both are dead, forty buckets suffice, and the lesser is included in the greater. If both are alive, they are removed and nothing is drawn. If the mouse is wounded or urinates, all the water is drawn. And does the well become pure with the last bucket when it separates from the water, or only when it is removed from the wellhead? According to Abu Yusuf, when it is removed from the wellhead; according to Muhammad, upon separation from the water. The practical difference: if water is taken from the well after separation from the water but before being removed from the wellhead, then according to Abu Yusuf it is impure, and according to Muhammad it is pure. Commentary
This passage presents rulings on purifying a well when a cat and mouse fall in simultaneously. The author first states the number of buckets to be drawn based on the state of the animals: if the cat is alive and the mouse dead, twenty buckets; if both dead, forty; if both alive, only removal; if the mouse is wounded or has urinated, all water must be drawn. A dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is then recorded regarding the moment of purification: Abu Yusuf requires the last bucket to be removed from the wellhead, while Muhammad considers purification upon separation from the water. This difference affects the ruling on water taken after separation but before removal from the wellhead—Abu Yusuf deems it impure, Muhammad pure. Another dispute concerns the case where the well dries out after the animal falls but before drawing: Abu Yusuf holds that when water returns, drawing is still required; Muhammad holds that drying purifies the well, allowing prayer at its bottom. Finally, if water recedes but the bottom remains wet, the correct view (according to later scholars) is that drawing is necessary despite Muhammad's earlier opinion. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi approach to resolving complex purification cases through rational analysis and reliance on established principles.

Key Terms
نَضَبَ (nadhaba): Water receding or seeping into the ground so that the well dries up.

Completing Well Purification When Water Runs Out: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on completing drawing when water runs out

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when water runs out during well purification and identify the difference between Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's positions.

Core Idea
When well water is depleted mid-drawing, the Hanafi school holds that the duty must be completed, but Muhammad analogizes to a dog to exempt further drawing.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario: twenty buckets are required for a well, but after drawing ten, the water runs out and is replaced by new water. Abu Yusuf insists on completing the full twenty, while Muhammad argues that no further drawing is needed, based on an analogy with a dog. Let us explore the reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the obligation
Recognize that the initial obligation is twenty buckets of water to be drawn from the well. The question is what happens when the water runs out after ten buckets and new water appears.
2
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that the remaining ten must be drawn to complete the duty. The fact that new water appeared does not cancel the obligation.
3
State the position of Muhammad
Muhammad holds that no further drawing is needed. He analogizes the situation to a dog falling into the well, which requires less water to purify.
The analogy is that the dog case is less severe, so if the dog case does not require extra drawing, then this case also should not.
4
Analyze the basis of the dispute
The difference hinges on whether the original obligation is tied to the well's state at the time of drawing or is a fixed number. Abu Yusuf sees the obligation as fixed; Muhammad sees it as contingent on the well's condition.
5
Identify the authority for the preferred view
The passage ends with 'كذا في الفتاوى', indicating later fatwa literature cites this ruling. The passage does not explicitly resolve the dispute, but Muhammad's view seems favored due to the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وجب في البئر نَزح، عشرين فنزح عشر فنفد الماء، ونبع غيره بعد ذلك : لزمهم عشر أخرى؛ تتميما للوظيفة عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد : لا يُحتاج إلى نزح شيء آخر؛ لأنه لا يكون أشد حالاً من الكلب. كذا في «الفتاوى». وهل تشترط المتابعة في النزح، أم لا؟ عندنا لا تشترط، وعند الحسن بن زياد : تشترط. قوله : ( بحَسَب كبر الحيوان وصُغره) : الكبر : بضم الكاف، وإسكان الباء للجنة، وكذا الصغر: بضم الصاد، وتسكين الغين. وأما بكسر الكاف وفتح الباء، وبكسر الصاد وفتح الغين: فللسن. ومعنى المسألة : إذا كان الواقع كبيراً ، أو البئر كبيرة: فالعشرة مستحبة. وإن كانا صغيرين: فالاستحباب دون ذلك. وإن كان أحدهما صغيراً، والآخر كبيراً: فخمس مستحبة، وخمس دونها في الاستحباب. قوله : (وإن ماتت فيها حمامة، أو دجاجةٌ، أو سنور: نُزح منـهـا مـا
If twenty buckets are obligatory in the well, then ten are drawn and the water runs out, and other water emerges after that: then an additional ten are necessary to complete the duty according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: No need to draw anything else, because it is not more severe than a dog. Thus in al-Fatawa. And is continuity required in drawing, or not? According to us (the Hanafi school), it is not required; according to al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, it is required. His statement: (According to the size of the animal and its smallness): 'al-kibr' with damm on the kaf and sukun on the ba refers to size, and likewise 'al-sighr' with damm on the sad and sukun on the ghayn. As for with kasr on the kaf and fatha on the ba, and with kasr on the sad and fatha on the ghayn, it refers to age. The meaning of the issue: if the animal that fell is large, or the well is large, then ten are recommended. If both are small, then the recommendation is less than that. If one is small and the other large, then five are recommended and five are below that in recommendation. His statement: (And if a pigeon, or a chicken, or a cat dies in it, some [water] is drawn from it...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on well purification with three additional issues. First, a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the case where twenty buckets were required but after drawing ten the water runs out and new water appears: Abu Yusuf holds that the remaining ten must be drawn to complete the duty, while Muhammad argues that no further drawing is needed, analogizing it to a dog (where a lesser amount suffices). Second, the issue of continuity (mutaba'ah) in drawing: the Hanafi school (at our time) does not require uninterrupted drawing, but al-Hasan ibn Ziyad holds it is required. Third, a clarification of the phrase 'according to the size of the animal and its smallness' in the matn: the author explains that the words 'kibr' and 'sighr' with particular vowel signs refer to size (large/small), while different vowels refer to age (old/young). The practical application is that the recommendation of drawing ten buckets varies: if the animal or well is large, ten are recommended; if both are small, fewer are recommended; if one is small and the other large, five are recommended. Finally, a new ruling is introduced: if a pigeon, chicken, or cat dies in the well, a certain amount (left incomplete) must be drawn.

Key Terms
متابعة (mutaba'ah): Continuity in drawing water from the well without interruption.
كبر / صغر (kibr / sighr): Size (large/small) of the animal or well, as opposed to age.

Continuity in Drawing Water: The Hanafi Position and a Dissenting View
Intra-Hanafi dispute on continuity in drawing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state whether continuity (mutaba'ah) is required in well purification and explain the evidence for the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school does not require uninterrupted drawing from a well; interruption does not invalidate the purification, unlike the view of Hasan ibn Ziyad.

A practical question arises: must a person draw water from a well continuously without interruption for well purification to be valid? The Hanafi school (at our time) holds that continuity is not a condition. However, Hasan ibn Ziyad, a notable Hanafi narrator, held that continuity is required. Let us examine the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point of dispute
The issue is whether the act of drawing water from a well must be performed continuously (mutaba'ah) without any break.
2
State the Hanafi school's position
The text uses 'عندنا' indicating the established ruling of our school: continuity is not required (لا تشترط).
3
State the opposing position
Hasan ibn Ziyad holds that continuity is required (تشترط).
Hasan ibn Ziyad is known for narrating rare opinions (nawadir) within the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the practical consequence
If a person interrupts the drawing (e.g., takes a break), according to the Hanafi school the purification remains valid; according to Hasan, it becomes invalid and must be restarted.
5
Note the authority of the Hanafi position
The word 'عندنا' signals that this is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position of the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وجب في البئر نَزح، عشرين فنزح عشر فنفد الماء، ونبع غيره بعد ذلك : لزمهم عشر أخرى؛ تتميما للوظيفة عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد : لا يُحتاج إلى نزح شيء آخر؛ لأنه لا يكون أشد حالاً من الكلب. كذا في «الفتاوى». وهل تشترط المتابعة في النزح، أم لا؟ عندنا لا تشترط، وعند الحسن بن زياد : تشترط. قوله : ( بحَسَب كبر الحيوان وصُغره) : الكبر : بضم الكاف، وإسكان الباء للجنة، وكذا الصغر: بضم الصاد، وتسكين الغين. وأما بكسر الكاف وفتح الباء، وبكسر الصاد وفتح الغين: فللسن. ومعنى المسألة : إذا كان الواقع كبيراً ، أو البئر كبيرة: فالعشرة مستحبة. وإن كانا صغيرين: فالاستحباب دون ذلك. وإن كان أحدهما صغيراً، والآخر كبيراً: فخمس مستحبة، وخمس دونها في الاستحباب. قوله : (وإن ماتت فيها حمامة، أو دجاجةٌ، أو سنور: نُزح منـهـا مـا
If twenty buckets are obligatory in the well, then ten are drawn and the water runs out, and other water emerges after that: then an additional ten are necessary to complete the duty according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: No need to draw anything else, because it is not more severe than a dog. Thus in al-Fatawa. And is continuity required in drawing, or not? According to us (the Hanafi school), it is not required; according to al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, it is required. His statement: (According to the size of the animal and its smallness): 'al-kibr' with damm on the kaf and sukun on the ba refers to size, and likewise 'al-sighr' with damm on the sad and sukun on the ghayn. As for with kasr on the kaf and fatha on the ba, and with kasr on the sad and fatha on the ghayn, it refers to age. The meaning of the issue: if the animal that fell is large, or the well is large, then ten are recommended. If both are small, then the recommendation is less than that. If one is small and the other large, then five are recommended and five are below that in recommendation. His statement: (And if a pigeon, or a chicken, or a cat dies in it, some [water] is drawn from it...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on well purification with three additional issues. First, a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the case where twenty buckets were required but after drawing ten the water runs out and new water appears: Abu Yusuf holds that the remaining ten must be drawn to complete the duty, while Muhammad argues that no further drawing is needed, analogizing it to a dog (where a lesser amount suffices). Second, the issue of continuity (mutaba'ah) in drawing: the Hanafi school (at our time) does not require uninterrupted drawing, but al-Hasan ibn Ziyad holds it is required. Third, a clarification of the phrase 'according to the size of the animal and its smallness' in the matn: the author explains that the words 'kibr' and 'sighr' with particular vowel signs refer to size (large/small), while different vowels refer to age (old/young). The practical application is that the recommendation of drawing ten buckets varies: if the animal or well is large, ten are recommended; if both are small, fewer are recommended; if one is small and the other large, five are recommended. Finally, a new ruling is introduced: if a pigeon, chicken, or cat dies in the well, a certain amount (left incomplete) must be drawn.

Key Terms
متابعة (mutaba'ah): Continuity in drawing water from the well without interruption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, not required
C) Only for large wells
D) Only for dogs

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not require continuity in drawing water for well purification; an interruption does not invalidate the process.

Understanding 'Kibr' and 'Sighr': Size Matters in Well Purification
Linguistic clarification and applied ruling on size-based recommendations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the words 'kibr' and 'sighr' for size vs. age and apply their meanings to determine recommended bucket draws.

Core Idea
The words 'kibr' and 'sighr' with damm refer to size of the animal or well, and the recommendation of ten buckets varies accordingly.

The matn of al-Quduri states that the amount of water to draw from a well is 'according to the size of the animal and its smallness'. The commentator clarifies that the words 'kibr' and 'sighr' with specific vowel signs refer to size, not age. This distinction affects whether ten buckets are recommended or a lesser amount. Let us examine the linguistic point and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase in the matn
The matn says: 'بحسب كبر الحيوان وصغره' (according to the size of the animal and its smallness). The commentator explains that 'kibr' and 'sighr' here refer to size (large/small), not age (old/young).
2
Learn the vowel signs for size vs. age
For size: 'كُبْر' (kibr) with damm on kaf and sukun on ba; 'صُغْر' (sighr) with damm on sad and sukun on ghayn. For age: 'كِبْر' (kibr) with kasr on kaf and fatha on ba; 'صِغْر' (sighr) with kasr on sad and fatha on ghayn.
This is a common Arabic homograph resolved by vowel signs.
3
Understand the ruling on recommended buckets
If the falling animal is large, or the well is large, then ten buckets are recommended (mustahabb). If both are small, the recommendation is less than ten. If one is small and the other large, five are recommended and five are less recommended.
The recommendation is based on size, not age.
4
Recognize the underlying principle
The size of the animal and well determines the extent of contamination and effort needed for purification. Larger animals or wells require more water.
5
Note the incomplete ruling for specific animals
The passage begins to state a ruling for a pigeon, chicken, or cat (specific amounts), but the text is cut off. In other parts of the work, the amounts are specified (e.g., 20, 40 buckets for a cat).

Amounts of Water to Remove from a Well (Nazh)
Explains water removal amounts based on animal/well size

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for the amount of water to remove from a well based on the size of the animal and the well, and explain the intra-Hanafi variation regarding the number of buckets for small animals.

Core Idea
Water removal from a well depends on size: large animal or well requires full removal; small animal and well requires partial removal (40-60 buckets).

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on purifying a well after an animal falls in. The amount of water to be removed (nazh) is not a fixed number; it varies based on the size of both the animal and the well. The matn of al-Quduri gives specific examples, and the commentary clarifies the underlying principle and an intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that removing water (nazh) is a purification requirement after impurity falls into a well. The ruling ranges from wajib (obligatory) to mustahabb (recommended) depending on the case.
2
Understand the size principle
The amount depends on whether the animal or well is large or small. If either is large, ten buckets are recommended; if both small, less than ten; if one small and one large, five strongly recommended and five less so.
This principle applies generally, but specific animals have fixed amounts.
3
Apply to specific animals
For pigeon, chicken, or cat: 40-60 buckets (or 50 according to al-Jami' al-Saghir). For dog, sheep, or human: all water must be removed.
The chicken (dajajah) is pronounced with fatha on the dal.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir says 50 buckets for the first category, while the matn gives a range of 40-60. The author considers 50 as the most apparent (al-azhar).
This difference does not affect the basic ruling but shows the ijtihad within the school.
5
Apply the rule for multiple animals
If two of the same small animal die (e.g., two cats, two chickens, two pigeons), all water must be removed, as the combined impurity is significant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَحَسَب كُبر الحيوان، وصُغره . قال في الصحاح : نضب الماء: أي غار في الأرض. قوله : ( بحَسَب كبر الحيوان وصُغره) : الكبر : بضم الكاف، وإسكان الباء للجنة، وكذا الصغر: بضم الصاد، وتسكين الغين. وأما بكسر الكاف وفتح الباء، وبكسر الصاد وفتح الغين: فللسن. ومعنى المسألة : إذا كان الواقع كبيراً ، أو البئر كبيرة: فالعشرة مستحبة. وإن كانا صغيرين: فالاستحباب دون ذلك. وإن كان أحدهما صغيراً، والآخر كبيراً: فخمس مستحبة، وخمس دونها في الاستحباب.
قوله : (وإن ماتت فيها حمامة، أو دجاجةٌ، أو سنور: نُزح منـهـا ما بين أربعين دلواً إلى ستين دلواً.
وإن مات فيها كلب، أو شاةٌ، أو آدمي : نُزح جميع مائها .
بين أربعين دلواً إلى ستين دلواً؛ إضعافاً للوجوب، والاستحباب في الفأرة.
وفي الجامع الصغير (۱): خمسون، وهو الأظهر؛ إضعافاً للوجوب، دون الاستحباب.
الدجاجة بفتح الدال على الأفصح، ويجوز كسرها، وهو شاذ، وأما ضمها : فخطأ.
وفي السنورين، والدجاجتين، والحمامتين : يُنزح كلُّ الماء.
According to the bigness of the animal and its smallness. He said in al-Sihah: 'nadaba al-ma'u' meaning it sank into the earth. His statement: 'According to the bigness of the animal and its smallness': 'al-kibr' with a damma on the kaf and sukun on the ba is for bigness, and likewise 'al-sughr' with a damma on the sad and sukun on the ghayn. As for with a kasra on the kaf and fatha on the ba, and with a kasra on the sad and fatha on the ghayn, that is for age. The meaning of the issue: If the fallen animal is large, or the well is large, then ten buckets are recommended. If both are small, then recommendation is less than that. If one is small and the other large, then five are recommended and five are less in recommendation.
His statement: 'If a pigeon, or a chicken, or a cat dies in it, between forty and sixty buckets are drawn from it.
And if a dog, or a sheep, or a human dies in it, all its water is drawn.
Between forty and sixty buckets; strengthening obligation and recommendation regarding the mouse.
And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: fifty, and it is the most apparent; strengthening obligation without recommendation.
The word 'dajajah' with fatha on the dal is most eloquent, and kasra is permissible but irregular; damma is a mistake.
And for two cats, two chickens, and two pigeons: all water is drawn.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn (Mukhtasar al-Quduri) followed by commentary. It establishes the principle that the required amount of water to be removed from a well after a dead animal falls in depends on both the size of the animal and the size of the well. The commentary first clarifies the Arabic terms: 'kibr' (bigness) and 'sughr' (smallness) are pronounced with a damma on the first letter and sukun on the second. If pronounced with kasra on the first and fatha on the second, it refers to age. The practical ruling: if either the animal or the well is large, it is recommended to remove ten buckets; if both are small, the recommendation is less; if one is small and the other large, five buckets are strongly recommended and five less so.

The matn then gives specific examples: for a pigeon, chicken, or cat, forty to sixty buckets are drawn. The commentary explains that this range is to strengthen both obligation and recommendation (for the mouse). Interestingly, a variant from al-Jami' al-Saghir (Imam Muhammad) says fifty buckets is the most apparent ruling, strengthening only obligation without recommendation. This is an intra-Hanafi difference on the precise number. The author (al-Hidayah or this sharh) prefers fifty as 'al-azhar' (most apparent).

Then a linguistic note: the word 'dajajah' (chicken) is correctly with fatha on the dal; kasra is irregular, and damma is incorrect.

Finally, the passage states that if two of any of these animals die (e.g., two cats, two chickens, two pigeons), all water must be removed. This is because the combined impurity is considered significant enough to require complete removal.

Key Terms
نَزْح (nazh): Drawing or removing water from the well by buckets.
كِبَر (kibar): Bigness or large size (of animal or well) – here with damma on kaf and sukun on ba.
دَجَاجَة (dajājah): A chicken; the word is correctly pronounced with fatha on the dal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) All water

Key Takeaway
The amount of water removed from a well after an animal dies depends on both the size of the animal and the well, with specific amounts for named animals and a general principle for other cases.

Well Purification: Exhaustion of Water and Continuity (Mutāba‘ah)
Expands on two intra-Hanafi disputes regarding well purification procedure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether continuity is required in well water removal and apply Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's rulings when water exhausts during removal.

Core Idea
Continuity is not required in well removal; exhaustion does not cancel remaining obligation according to Abu Yusuf.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira presents two intra-Hanafi disputes related to the practical process of purifying a well after a dead animal is removed. The first concerns what happens when the water in the well runs dry mid-way through the required removal, and new water appears. The second asks whether the removal must be performed continuously without interruption. These questions directly affect the fiqh of tahārah when dealing with wells.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first dispute: exhaustion of water
The passage states that if twenty buckets are required and after ten the water runs out, then new water appears. You must determine whether the remaining ten must still be removed.
The two positions are from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Extract the reasoning for each position
Abu Yusuf says the remaining ten must be removed to 'complete the task' (tatmīman lil-waẓīfa). Muhammad says no further removal needed, arguing by analogy that the situation is no more severe than the case of a dog (implying a less strict requirement).
Note that Muhammad's proof is qiyās to the dog case.
3
Identify the second dispute: continuity (mutāba‘ah)
The text asks directly: is continuity required? The school's position (indanā) is no; al-Hasan ibn Ziyad requires it.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school itself.
4
Recognize the authority weight
The school's view (no continuity) is the established position; Hasan ibn Ziyad's view is from the nawādir (rare transmissions). The text mentions the exhaustion dispute is recorded in al-Fatāwā.
Knowing authority weight helps in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وجب في البئر نَزح ،عشرين فنزح عشر فنفد الماء، ونبع غيره بعد ذلك : لزمهم عشر أخرى ؛ تتميما للوظيفة عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد : لا يُحتاج إلى نزح شيء آخر؛ لأنه لا يكون أشد حالاً من الكلب. كذا في «الفتاوى».
وهل تشترط المتابعة في النزح، أم لا؟
عندنا لا تشترط، وعند الحسن بن زياد : تشترط.
قوله : (فإن مات فيها كلب، أو شاة، أو دابةٌ، أو آدمي: تُرح جميع مائها).
موت الكلب ليس بشرط، حتى لو خرج حياً: يُنزح جميع الماء. وكذا كل ما سؤره نجس، أو مشكوك فيه: يجب نزح الكل وإن خرج حياً.
وما سؤره مكروه : إذا خرج حياً : فالماء مكروه، يُنزح منه عشر دلاء.
والشاة إذا خرجت حيةً ولم تكن هاربة من السبع: فالماء طاهر، وإن كانت هاربةً : نُزح كل الماء عندهما، خلافاً لمحمد.
And if twenty bucketfuls of removal were required from the well, and they removed ten and the water ran out, and other water gushed forth afterwards, then it is necessary for them to remove another ten; to complete the task according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: No need to remove anything else, because it is not more severe than the dog. Thus in al-Fatawa.
And is continuity required in the removal, or not?
In our school, it is not required, and according to al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, it is required.
His statement: 'If a dog, or a sheep, or an animal, or a human dies in it, all its water is removed.'
The death of the dog is not a condition, so even if it comes out alive, all the water is removed. And likewise everything whose leftover water is najis or doubtful: removal of all is obligatory even if it comes out alive.
And that whose leftover water is makruh: if it comes out alive, the water is makruh, and ten bucketfuls are removed from it.
And the sheep: if it comes out alive and was not fleeing from a predator, then the water is pure; and if it was fleeing, then all water is removed according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Muhammad (al-Shaybani).
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents three intra-Hanafi disputes related to well purification.

First dispute: If a required removal of twenty buckets is begun but after ten buckets the water runs out, and then new water appears, what should be done? According to Abu Yusuf, the remaining ten must still be removed to complete the obligation, because the purpose is to remove the impurity of the dead animal, and the new water does not negate that. According to Imam Muhammad, no additional removal is needed because once the water has been exhausted and new water appears, the situation is similar to the case of a dog: if a dog falls in and is removed alive, only some water is required? Actually Muhammad's reasoning: 'it is not more severe than the dog' – meaning that the new water is considered pure enough. The text notes that this ruling is recorded in al-Fatawa.

Second dispute: Is continuity (mutaba'ah) required in the removal of water? The majority of the Hanafi school (our school) says continuity is not required; the removal can be done intermittently. However, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (a Hanafi scholar known for rare transmissions) requires continuity.

Third dispute: The commentary explains the ruling when animals come out alive from the well. For a dog, even if it comes out alive, all water must be removed because the dog's saliva and body are considered najis. Similarly, any animal whose leftover water (su'r) is najis or doubtful requires removal of all water even if it comes out alive. If the leftover water is makruh (disliked), and the animal comes out alive, then the water is makruh and only ten buckets are removed. Finally, for a sheep: if it comes out alive and was not fleeing from a predator, the water is pure (no removal needed). But if it was fleeing, then according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two, i.e., the Sahibayn), all water must be removed; however, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees (khilafan li-Muhammad), possibly holding that the water remains pure even if fleeing. The precise position of Muhammad here is not elaborated in this text.

Key Terms
مُتَابَعَة (mutāba‘ah): Continuity in the removal of water; performing the removal without interruption.
سُؤْر (su’r): The leftover water of an animal (usually refers to water left after drinking, but here: the water in the well after contact with the animal).
هَارِبَة (hāribah): Fleeing (from a predator); a sheep that escapes from a predator into the well is considered to be in a state of impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is continuity required in well water removal?
A) Yes, it is wajib.
B) No, it is not required.
C) It is mustahab.
D) It is makruh.

Key Takeaway
Continuity is not required in well removal, and Abu Yusuf's position on exhaustion mandates completion of the full quota even after water renewal.

Well Purification: Animal Coming Out Alive and Categorization by Su'r
Expands on rulings for well purification when animals come out alive, with an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the well purification rules for animals that come out alive based on their su'r, and state the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a fleeing sheep.

Core Idea
The ruling on water when an animal comes out alive depends on the status of its su'r; for a sheep fleeing a predator, all water must be removed according to the Sahibayn.

The final part of this passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira addresses a crucial practical scenario: an animal falls into a well and is removed alive. The ruling varies dramatically based on the type of animal and whether it was fleeing a predator. This section also reveals a less-known intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the one hand, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animals and their su'r categories
The text lists: dog (su'r najis), animals with najis or doubtful su'r (must remove all water even if alive), animals with makruh su'r (remove 10 bucketfuls, water becomes makruh), and sheep (pure unless fleeing).
Recall that su'r is the leftover water/drops from the animal's mouth.
2
Understand the special exception for sheep
A sheep that falls in not fleeing: water is pure and no removal needed. If it was fleeing from a predator, it is considered like a dead animal, so all water must be removed according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
This is based on the principle that a frightened animal may have released najasah due to stress.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
The text says 'according to the two (عندهما)' meaning Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, contrary to Muhammad (خلافاً لمحمد). There is confusion: some scholars interpret this as a dispute between the Sahibayn and Muhammad al-Shaybani himself, or between two narrations from Muhammad. The tarjih is not given.
See the analytical toolbox for more.
4
Extract the practical implication
If a sheep comes out alive and was fleeing, the mufti must decide which view to follow. The majority practice follows the Sahibayn.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether all water is removed or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وجب في البئر نَزح ،عشرين فنزح عشر فنفد الماء، ونبع غيره بعد ذلك : لزمهم عشر أخرى ؛ تتميما للوظيفة عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد : لا يُحتاج إلى نزح شيء آخر؛ لأنه لا يكون أشد حالاً من الكلب. كذا في «الفتاوى».
وهل تشترط المتابعة في النزح، أم لا؟
عندنا لا تشترط، وعند الحسن بن زياد : تشترط.
قوله : (فإن مات فيها كلب، أو شاة، أو دابةٌ، أو آدمي: تُرح جميع مائها).
موت الكلب ليس بشرط، حتى لو خرج حياً: يُنزح جميع الماء. وكذا كل ما سؤره نجس، أو مشكوك فيه: يجب نزح الكل وإن خرج حياً.
وما سؤره مكروه : إذا خرج حياً : فالماء مكروه، يُنزح منه عشر دلاء.
والشاة إذا خرجت حيةً ولم تكن هاربة من السبع: فالماء طاهر، وإن كانت هاربةً : نُزح كل الماء عندهما، خلافاً لمحمد.
And if twenty bucketfuls of removal were required from the well, and they removed ten and the water ran out, and other water gushed forth afterwards, then it is necessary for them to remove another ten; to complete the task according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: No need to remove anything else, because it is not more severe than the dog. Thus in al-Fatawa.
And is continuity required in the removal, or not?
In our school, it is not required, and according to al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, it is required.
His statement: 'If a dog, or a sheep, or an animal, or a human dies in it, all its water is removed.'
The death of the dog is not a condition, so even if it comes out alive, all the water is removed. And likewise everything whose leftover water is najis or doubtful: removal of all is obligatory even if it comes out alive.
And that whose leftover water is makruh: if it comes out alive, the water is makruh, and ten bucketfuls are removed from it.
And the sheep: if it comes out alive and was not fleeing from a predator, then the water is pure; and if it was fleeing, then all water is removed according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Muhammad (al-Shaybani).
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents three intra-Hanafi disputes related to well purification.

First dispute: If a required removal of twenty buckets is begun but after ten buckets the water runs out, and then new water appears, what should be done? According to Abu Yusuf, the remaining ten must still be removed to complete the obligation, because the purpose is to remove the impurity of the dead animal, and the new water does not negate that. According to Imam Muhammad, no additional removal is needed because once the water has been exhausted and new water appears, the situation is similar to the case of a dog: if a dog falls in and is removed alive, only some water is required? Actually Muhammad's reasoning: 'it is not more severe than the dog' – meaning that the new water is considered pure enough. The text notes that this ruling is recorded in al-Fatawa.

Second dispute: Is continuity (mutaba'ah) required in the removal of water? The majority of the Hanafi school (our school) says continuity is not required; the removal can be done intermittently. However, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (a Hanafi scholar known for rare transmissions) requires continuity.

Third dispute: The commentary explains the ruling when animals come out alive from the well. For a dog, even if it comes out alive, all water must be removed because the dog's saliva and body are considered najis. Similarly, any animal whose leftover water (su'r) is najis or doubtful requires removal of all water even if it comes out alive. If the leftover water is makruh (disliked), and the animal comes out alive, then the water is makruh and only ten buckets are removed. Finally, for a sheep: if it comes out alive and was not fleeing from a predator, the water is pure (no removal needed). But if it was fleeing, then according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two, i.e., the Sahibayn), all water must be removed; however, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees (khilafan li-Muhammad), possibly holding that the water remains pure even if fleeing. The precise position of Muhammad here is not elaborated in this text.

Key Terms
مُتَابَعَة (mutāba‘ah): Continuity in the removal of water; performing the removal without interruption.
سُؤْر (su’r): The leftover water of an animal (usually refers to water left after drinking, but here: the water in the well after contact with the animal).
هَارِبَة (hāribah): Fleeing (from a predator); a sheep that escapes from a predator into the well is considered to be in a state of impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling for a dog that comes out alive from a well?
A) All water must be removed.
B) Ten bucketfuls must be removed.
C) The water is pure.
D) No removal needed because it is alive.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on well water when an animal comes out alive varies by su'r category; a fleeing sheep requires full water removal according to the Sahibayn.

Removing Water from a Well for a Dead Mouse (Hanafi Ruling)
Ruling statement with expansion probe

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the number of buckets to remove for a dead mouse in a well and identify the preferred amount from al-Jami' al-Saghir.

Core Idea
For a mouse in a well, remove 40–60 buckets, with 50 being the most apparent preferred amount.

The Hanafi school provides specific guidance for purifying well water when an animal dies in it. This lesson covers the case of a mouse as stated in al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, along with the commentator's expansion from al-Jami' al-Saghir and a linguistic note on the word 'dajajah' (hen). Understanding this ruling is essential for maintaining ritual purity (taharah) in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is removing water from a well after a mouse dies in it. The ruling is wajib (obligatory) due to the phrase 'إضعافاً للوجوب' (to emphasize the obligation).
The term 'wajib' is standard for Hanafi rulings derived from zanni evidence.
2
Note the range from the matn
Al-Quduri's matn states between 40 and 60 buckets. This range is given to strengthen the obligation for the specific case of a mouse.
The range is not arbitrary; it serves a legal purpose.
3
Recognize the preferred amount
Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir specifies exactly 50 buckets, which the commentator labels 'الأظهر' (the most apparent) position.
This represents the mufta bihi (operative) ruling.
4
Understand the linguistic note
The commentator corrects the pronunciation of 'دجاجة' (hen) – fatha on dāl is correct, kasra is permissible, but damm is an error.
This side note is part of the commentary tradition, ensuring accurate reading of fiqh texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين أربعين دلواً إلى ستين دلواً. بين أربعين دلواً إلى ستين دلواً؛ إضعافاً للوجوب، والاستحباب في الفأرة. وفي الجامع الصغير: خمسون، وهو الأظهر؛ إضعافاً للوجوب، دون الاستحباب. الدجاجة بفتح الدال على الأفصح، ويجوز كسرها، وهو وأما ضمها : فخطاً. شاد،
Between forty buckets to sixty buckets. Between forty buckets to sixty buckets; doubling the obligation, and the recommendation for the mouse. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: fifty, and it is the most apparent; doubling the obligation, but not the recommendation. 'Al-dajajah' with fath of the dal is more eloquent, and its kasr is permissible, and as for its damm, it is an error. Shad,
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling for a mouse that dies in a well. The matn states a range of forty to sixty buckets of water to be removed. The commentator explains that this range is given to strengthen (i.e., emphasize) the obligation for the mouse case, while the recommended amount is also within this range. However, in al-Jami' al-Saghir, the preferred number is fifty, which is the apparent position. This number is for strengthening the obligation, but not the recommendation (i.e., for the mouse, the mandatory amount is higher, but the recommended amount is not increased). The commentator then gives a linguistic note on the word 'dajajah' (hen), clarifying its correct pronunciation: it is with fatha on the dal, though kasra is permissible, but damm is incorrect. The word 'shad' at the end is unclear but may be a scribal error.

Key Terms
الدجاجة (al-dajajah): The hen, in the context of animals that may fall into a well, requiring a specific amount of water to be removed.
الأظهر (al-azhar): The most apparent or preferred opinion among competing transmissions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the most apparent number of buckets to remove for a mouse dead in a well?
A) 40
B) 60
C) 50
D) between 40 and 60

Key Takeaway
For a mouse in a well, the Hanafi ruling is to remove between 40 and 60 buckets, with 50 being the most apparent preferred amount; additionally, the word 'dajajah' is correctly pronounced with fatha on the dāl.

Water in a Well: Rulings for Dead Animals and Intra-Hanafi Disputes
States the ruling that all water must be removed if a dog, sheep, or human dies in a well, including details about animals coming out alive and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for water in a well when a dog, sheep, or human dies, distinguish the scenarios of animals coming out alive, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute about a fleeing sheep.

Core Idea
Even if a dog comes out alive, its najis saliva makes all well water impure.

Water in a well can become impure if an animal dies or falls in. The ruling depends on the type of animal and whether its saliva is pure, najis, or makruh. This lesson covers the detailed Hanafi rules, including an important dispute between the Sahibayn and Imam Muhammad regarding a sheep that escaped a predator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal and its saliva category
Determine if the animal's su'r (residual saliva) is pure, najis, or makruh. Dog saliva is najis; sheep saliva is normally pure but can become problematic if the sheep was fleeing.
The categories of su'r are key to the ruling.
2
Determine if the animal died or came out alive
If the animal dies, all water is removed for certain animals. If alive, the ruling varies by saliva category and specific conditions.
Death is not required for dogs and some other animals.
3
Apply the removal rule based on saliva
For najis su'r, complete removal is wajib. For makruh su'r, ten buckets are removed. For pure su'r, no removal unless the animal was fleeing from a predator.
The amount of water to remove differs by category.
4
Handle the special case of a fleeing sheep
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) say all water must be removed; Imam Muhammad in a solitary transmission disagrees.
The text presents the view of the two as the relied-upon position.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi position
The commentator indicates that the view of the Sahibayn is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Other transmissions exist, but this is the strongest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن مات فيها كلب، أو شاةٌ، أو آدمي : نُزح جميع مائها. * قوله : (فإن مات فيها كلب، أو شاة، أو دابةٌ، أو آدمي: تُرح جميع مائها). موت الكلب ليس بشرط، حتى لو خرج حياً: يُنزح جميع الماء. وكذا كل ما سؤره نجس، أو مشكوك فيه: يجب نزح الكل وإن خرج حياً. وما سؤره مكروه : إذا خرج حياً : فالماء مكروه، يُنزح منه عشر دلاء. والشاة إذا خرجت حيةً ولم تكن هاربة من السبع: فالماء طاهر، وإن كانت هاربةً : نُزح كل الماء عندهما، خلافاً لمحمد. (١) في الجامع الصغير لمحمد بن الحسن ص ٦٥ : أربعون، أو خمسون.
And if a dog, or a sheep, or a human dies in it: all of its water is removed. He said: (If a dog, or a sheep, or a beast, or a human dies in it: all of its water is removed). The death of the dog is not a condition, so even if it comes out alive: all the water is removed. Likewise, every animal whose residual saliva is najis or doubtful: complete removal is obligatory even if it comes out alive. And one whose residual saliva is makruh: if it comes out alive: the water is makruh, and ten buckets are removed from it. And the sheep, if it comes out alive and was not fleeing from a predator: the water is pure, but if it was fleeing: all the water is removed according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Muhammad. (1) In al-Jami' al-Saghir by Muhammad ibn al-Hasan p. 65: forty or fifty.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a dog, sheep, or human dies in a well. The ruling is that all water must be removed. Importantly, for a dog, death is not a condition – even if it comes out alive, all water must be removed because its saliva is najis. Similarly, any animal with najis or doubtful saliva requires complete removal. For animals with makruh saliva, if they come out alive, the water becomes makruh, and only ten buckets need to be removed. For a sheep, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: if it comes out alive but was not fleeing a predator, the water remains pure; if it was fleeing, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn) say all water must be removed, while Imam Muhammad alone (in one report) differs. The footnote indicates that in al-Jami' al-Saghir, the ruling for sheep is forty or fifty buckets, which might be a different transmission.

Key Terms
سؤر (su'r): The residual saliva or moisture left after an animal drinks from a vessel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a dog falls into a well and comes out alive?
A) All water is pure
B) Ten buckets are removed
C) All water must be removed
D) No water is removed

Key Takeaway
When an animal with najis saliva enters a well, all water must be removed regardless of whether it dies or comes out alive.
